SEBASTIAN CARTER

# Twentieth century type designers

Sebastian Carter

The skeleton shapes of the letters of our alphabet change hardly at all, yet many skilled type designers have devoted much time, sometimes their whole lives, to drawing different versions of the outlines. This book is written for the general reader who is aware that types differ, and would like to know more about how and why, and about the personalities who created them.

Sebastian Carter here examines the careers of a number of type designers working in this century, against the background of the enormous changes in the mehods of reating and setting type, from meal characters composed by hand to the computerized teehnology of today. The introduction in Ameriea, a hundred years ago, of three machines — the Linotype and Monotype hot-metal composing machines, and the Benton pantographic punchcutter — revolutionized the manufacture of type, and the Benton machine in particular caused a flood of new faces by designers, most of whom could not cut a puneh by hand. More recently, filmsetting and digital typesetting have brought new opportunities as well as new disciplines.

The designers of this century have worked from widely different motives, ranging from a practical need to produce faees for new machines to an idealistie attempt to create perfect letterforms for our age. This volume looks at the work of such dedicated master craftsmen as Goudy, Rogers, Koch, Dwiggins, Gill, Morison, Mardersteig, Van Krimpen, Trump, Tschichold, Excoffon, Zapf, Frutiger and others, as well as recognizing the contributions of skilled technicians such as Benton, who redrew and improved many existing types. And it outlines the adaptation of classic designs of the past to the various changes in typesetting technology, notably in the series of recuttings undertaken between the world wars by the English Monotype Corporation.

# Sources and acknowledgements

The printed literature on type design is scanty and does not generally stay long in print. There are broadly speaking two classes of publication. The first includes specimen books, type-finders and other compendious gatherings, which reproduce alphabets but tend to give little information beyond, sometimes, the designer and date, and telling the reader what is plainly visible: for instancc, that the capital H has a high cross bar, the lower-case g an unclosed tail and the t is tall. This example is actually taken from the best-known publication of its kind, The encyclopaedia of type faces, by W. Pincus Jaspert, W. Turncr Bcrry and A. F. Johnson, London, Blandford Press, 1953 (4th edition 1970), distributed in the USA by Sterling Publishing Inc, Ncw York. It is extremely uscful for quick rcfcrenee and identification, but sometimes incomplete in such detail as it gives.

The second class consists of books on groups of designers or individuals. Most of these are out of print, some are scaree even in the second-hand market, and some are extremely valuable: it is a specialised field, and the standards of printing are naturally high. I have given the details of sueh books where they are referred to in the text, and noted the cases where they have been reissued in paperback.

Particularly useful among the group studies are A talhy of types (Cambridgc University Press, 1973), Stanley Morison's account of the Monotype recuttings in which he had some say or were madc while he was their adviser (the frstiton was a lmited one, which eame out in 1953 as a Cambridge Christmas book; the 1973 edition is enlarged, with a particularly useful new introduction by P. M. Handover); and Joseph Blumenthal's The printed book in America (Boston, David Godine, 1977; London, Scolar Press). Walter Tracy's book Letters of credit (London, Gordon Fraser, 1986) eame out too late for me to consult, though I am grateful to Peter Guy of Gordon Frascr for rushing me an advance copy before this book wcnt to press.

Some individual designers have been written about well, usually, though not always, on the occasion of a birthday. I have formed a rough rule of thumb in assessing books published to celebrate such occasions, which states that the more grandiose a Festschrift is, the less there is of useful material in it. The enormous folio produced for the fiftieth birthday of the German artist Emil Rudolf Weiss in 1925 is a case in point: magnificently illustrated, full of reverential tributes and poems by friends and colleagues, it is almost completely devoid of information on its subject. Thc far more modest octavo brought out in 1983 by the Darmstadt Technieal College with the Maximilian Gesellschaft to celebrate Hermann Zapf's sixty-fifth birthday is equally wcll illustrated within its smaller format, but is also, by contrast with the Weiss book, a very useful working tool for the biographer. Other exemplary works are John Dreyfus's The work of Fan van Krimpen (London, Sylvan Press, 1952), Nieolas Barkcr's biography Stanley Morison (London, Macmillan, 1972), Georg Haupt's Rudolf Koch der Schreiber (Leipzig, Insel Verlag, 1936), and the collections of essays Victor Hammer, artist and printer (Lexington, Anvil Press, 1981) and Robert Hunter Middleton, the man and his letters (Chieago, Caxton Club, 1985).

Finally, there are the writings of the designers themselvcs. Not all of them have written well on their work, and some have written nothing. But we are fortunate in having Dwiggins's WAD to RR, a letter abont designing type (Harvard College Library, Cambridge, 194o); Frutiger's Type, sign, symbol (Zurich, ABC Vcrlag, 198o); Goudy's A half-centnry of type design and typography (New York, The Typophiles, 1946) and Typologia (Berkeley, University of California Press, 194o); Van Krimpen's On designing and devising type (New York, The Typophiles, 1957); and Zapf's About alphabets (New York, The Typophiles, 1960), and Hunt Roman, the birth of a type (The Pittsburgh Bibliophiles, 1965).

Several periodicals haveincludeartiles ntypfaees: The Fleuron, The Peurose aunnal, Signature, Typography and Alphabet and image among the defunet, and among the current ones Fine print (San Franeisco), Matrix (Andoversford, England) and Visible lauguage Cleveland, Ohio; formerly called Fournal of typographic research). There are also the house journals The Monototype recorder, Linotype matrix and the International Typeface Corporation's Uc. For English students, the St Bride Printing Library's subjeet index to periodical artieles is invaluable, and the staff extremely helpful.

During the writing of this book I beeame quite ruthless about picking the brains of the experts. Among those who submitted with inexplicable good humour to my inquisitions were Nicolas Barker, Brucc Beck, Joseph Blumenthal, Carolyn Hammer, William Hesterberg, Herbert Johnson, Abe Lerner, Martino Mardersteig, David MeKitterick, James Mosley, David Pankow, Walter Tracy, Gerard Unger, Laurence Wallis, Berthold Wolpe and Hermann Zapf. Mueh help was given by John Latimer Smith of Trefoil Books, and I owe a particular debt to John Dreyfus, for the loan of background material and for many correetions of detail. The index was compiled by Penny Carter. None of these, of course, can be held responsible for any errors which have crept through, for which I must take the blame.

For permission to quote from various writings, I am grateful to the following: for Stanley Morison, Brooke Crutehley; for Jan van Krimpen, Huib van Krimpen; for Frederic Goudy, the University of California Press, and the Typophiles, New York.

I ought to declare a family interest. I am the son of Will Carter, the designer of two Monotype faces, Klang and Óctavian (the latter with David Kindersley), whose collection of books on typography has provided much illustrative material; but I am no relation to Matthew Carter, the designer of Olympian, Galliard and other types. Finally, it is conventional to thank one's family for their forebearance during the writing of a book, though at the beginning my wife and children had no more idea than I of how much forebearanee would be needed. I am grateful to them for still bcing around at the end.

Sebastian Carter Cambridge, November 1986

The skeleton shapes of the letters of our alphabet ehange hardly at all. Why then do skilled type designers devote so mueh time, sometimes their whole lives, to drawing different versions of the outlines?

The French designer Roger Exeoffon likened the basie letter to a road, and the designers to drivers, each of whom steers a slightly different route along it. But this analogy does not explain why these drivers get into their ears in the first place.

Rudolf Koch, the German ealligrapher and type designer, wrote: 'The making of letters in every form is for me the purest and the greatest pleasure, and at many stages of my life it was to me what a song is to the singer, a pieture to the painter, a shout to the elated, or a sigh to the oppressed  it was and is for me the most happy and perfeet expression of my life.' By no means all the designers mentioned in this book operated at this level of ereative eestasy, but all were driven by a love of letters and a burning desire to add refinements to the common stock of printing types.

Anyone interested in type today is eonfronted with a bewildering variety of faces in books and magazines, although the choice available to the typographer from any one printer or typesetter is usually quite limited. In many ways types are like wines: one ean learn to diseriminate between the varieties of Garamond as produeed by Monotype, Linotype, ATF or Stempel, just as the wine taster can deteet the Chardonnay grape, whatever vineyard it eomes from. For most purposes the wine drinker may bc eonfined to the stock of his local merchant, but his selection is the better for a wider knowledge of the whole subjeet.

Until recently, any account of the development of type de tok it r aned that he letts wuld be cut in relief, and east in metal. Now, with the dramatie changes in typesetting technology whieh have taken plaee sinee World War II, we must redefine what a typeface is. It is no longer necessarily likely to be a digitised message stored in a computer memory. But redefinition crcates broad eategories: if we say that a typeface is a standardised letter form intendcd for repetitious assembly and for reproduetion in print, we must admit the output of typewriters, word-proeessors and eomputer printers, since manuals and even books are often printed from them. Sinee this is not a long book, I shall lcave these workaday alphabets tacitly on onc side, togcther with typewriter imitations of successful typefaces. For the same reason, I shall devote less spaee than I would like to designs for display faees, whether for metal type or dry-transfer lettering.

A question often asked by the publie is whethcr we need so many new types. A glance at the specimen books, both new and old, suggests that many are issued whieh we eertainly do not. But the produetion of new faces is given impetus by two forees which reason not the need. One is the thirst on the part of the users, both typographers and their elients, partieularly in advertising, for novelty and cxclusiveness. The desire for novclty in an cxtremc form ean lead to the worst exeesses of the dry-transfcr repertoire, but in itself is normal and blameless: we feel it in a milder form in book types. For all the declarations of the more puritanical theorists, most type-conscious pcople feel that a typeface that has becomc very familiar does not necessarily bccome 'invisible', and therefore a commendable means of conveying a writer's thought: it often just beeomes boring. Even Adrian Frutiger, the designer of Univers, a sans-serif intended for widc acceptance and use because of its carefully detailed unobtrusiveness, eonfessed that its very suecess made it secm dated twenty years later.

The wish for an exclusive faee convinces hardencd business men, from ear manufaeturcrs to the heads of publie utilities, to eommission special alphabcts for corporate use. On the oeeasions when these eommissions throw up types suitablc for wider usc, such as

Times New Roman, Centaur or Roissy (Frutiger), most patrons are glad to allow them to be publiely released. Certainly few have gone as far as Cobden-Sanderson, who threw the Doves Press type off Hammersmith Bridge into the Thames rather than allow it to be used by anyone else.

The other irresistible foree for change in type design is the reeiproeal need of the manufaeturers of typesetting equipment to show a range of faees whieh will persuade everyone to buy their maehines and no one clse's. Between the two world wars the struggle was prineipally between Linotype and Monotype, but with the eoming of filmsetting and later developments in 'cold' eomposition, many more systems arrived to eompete in the market. A number of the established foundries, also, who made only metal type for hand setting, vied for the market in display sizes with an adventurous poliey of issuing new designs. All the reputable firms built up highly skilled drawing offices to adapt both existing faces and new designs for the teehnieal demands of their partieular system, and the house designers of sueh offiees are the largely unsung heroes of the business, responsible for many elassie types being sueeessfully transferred to mechanieal eomposition or casting, as well as giving neeessary guidanee to new designs on their way to realisation. Men sueh as Sol Hess of the Ameriean Lanston Monotype Company, Morris Benton of Ameriean Type Founders and Fritz Steltzer of English Monotype spent mueh of their working lives redrawing other people's designs to the speeifie requirements of their maehines or keeping their eharaeteristies intaet while developing them into type families.

Sueh work is inevitably, beeause intended to be, undetectable by the untrained eye, and a lot of it goes into keeping paee with teehnologieal ehange. As an example of how the various forees so far deseribed eombine to produee a workhorse type, there is the case of Bell Centennial. Up to 1937, the telephone direetories in the United States were printed locally in whatever type the printer ehose; but in that year the Bell company asked C. H. Griffith of Mergenthaler Linotype to design a face for use in all their directories. A eondensed sans-serif was produeed, ealled Bell Gothic (English readers should note that 'gothie' is American for sans-serif), whieh served well until the printers switched their composition over from hot-metal Linotypes to eathode ray tube typesetters in the early 197os. The new method's coarser resolution led to some loss of detail and to complaints from subscribers. At the same time, AT&T, who now owned Bell, had a new corporatc graphic style designed by Saul Bass, and thought that Bell Gothic lookcd too dated to fit in with it. Linotype, who made the CRT machines now being used, were approached again and asked to rcdcsign the face for the ncw conditions. Their designer Matthew Carter made the necessary changcs, and in 1978 Bell Centennial came into use. Comparison between the old and new pagcs shows that the new directorics are far more legible, and the complaints were stilled; but otherwise they look much the same. A great deal of time and cnergy had been spent by experts in making what was mcant to be an unnoticcable improvemcnt, and this is true of by far the greatcst proportion of thc efforts of type designers.

But side by side with such deliberately subtle tcchnicians, somctimes cven among the same peoplc, thcre is another group of dcsigners. These produce the original faces whose characteristics, even if redrawn by the drawing offices, survive translation. They profoundly change the shapes of the letters we usc. It is they who are the main subject of this book.

It is not an encyclopacdia of all the designers of the century, though I hope it mentions all the major ones in the Wcst. It aims to put faces to namcs: many users of typc can tell Gill from Goudy on the page, but know little of thc men who created the types (I have even bcen asked if Goudy was the samc man as the Spanish architect Gaudi). It also tries to show how the designers worked, what they thought they were doing, and how they reacted to their contcmporaries; and it attcmpts to set them against the background of the changing demands of a rapidly evolving industry. In order to make this background as clcar as possible, the story opens with a brief outline of the developmcnt of printing and type design up to the turn of the century.

## Der Schriftgieer.

![](images/508c984f8344d9df136f1e06bd91b9989a875fe47b8c06c34824e46ba4ffc2d1.jpg)

Chinese compositors. An illustration from a manual on printing from moveable types wrillen by Chin Chien in 1777

As far as we know, the Chinese were the first to print books from moveable type. In the middle of the eleventh century one Pi Cheng apparently worked with re-usable eeramic eharacters, though no examples of his work survive. Then, early in the fourteenth eentury, Wang Chen wrote that, since Pi Cheng, types had been cast in tin but had not been successful because Chinese water-based inks did not take readily to metal; he went on to describe his own work with wooden type. Bronze eharaeters cast from wooden masters impressed into sand moulds were later used extensively in Korea, still before the introduction of printing to Europe around the middle of the fifteenth century, but the huge number of eharacters in the Chinese and Korean languages meant that typographie books never seriously challenged works printed from hand-eut wood-blocks. In Europe, whose alphabetieal system of writing required relatively few different symbols, the position was reversed.

We do not know if Johannes Gutenberg of Mainz, generally accepted as the father of the European printing revolution, knew of Chinese printing, though he relied heavily on another Chinese invention, the making of paper. His aehievement was in the bringing together of existing teehniques, the serew press, oilbased pigments, and above all the metal-working skills of punch-eutting (previously developed for patterning metal) and easting to make possible the manufaeture of type, and it was a major aehievement. Quite soon after he started experimenting, he produeed his first book, the 'Forty-two-line Bible' (eirea 1455), whieh remains one of the most beautiful books ever printed; while the process he developed remained unehanged in its essentials for over four eenturies.

In outline, the way the process worked was thus, beginning with the manufaeture of the type. A punch was eut by hand for eaeh letter of the alphabet needed, in every size and style, and for punctuation. It eonsisted of the letter cut in relief, and in reverse, on the end of a peg of steel. This puneh was then struek with a mallet, to exactly the same depth as its fellows, into a small bar of copper, the strike, whieh was trimmed into the matrix by the teehniques of justification. For although types (with regional variations) are of equal height-to-paper, and the bodies of any one size are the same from head to foot, the letters vary in width, from the narrow I to the wide m. The moulds into whieh the matriees were fitted to east the pieces of type were therefore made of two parts whieh were slotted together and were adjustable to the width of the letter; and the two halves were held apart to the correct width by the body of the matrix. The justification of the matrix was consequently of great importance in the appearance of the typc on the paper, because it determined the closeness of the letters to their neighbours, and the evenness of the spacing between them, which are just as important as the shapes of the letters themselves.

![](images/c2c4bd5468a86c2d1cbe2c85e6e8c181d5983e56cac91d75af577f0cf4c91f69.jpg)

![](images/a44ffb48cacd130f3ccf98b2a0e26ae21d8a9d3a77aa7fbae9acca13d8677111.jpg)  
The punch, the strike, the justified matrix, and type; and the type mould.

Punches were the form in which the master design of a typeface was preserved, and could not be exactly copied. Cutting them was a craft of great skill, and sets of punches were highly prized, not least because they could be used to produce many sets of matrices. Indeed, it is arguable that several early types which were once thought to be different designs were in fact struck from the same punches, with maybe one or two letters substituted; but the matrices were justificd with greater or lesser finesse, and the type printcd with heavier or lighter impression.

Whereas the early punchcutters were usually independent craftsmen who would produce sets of punches or matrices to order, casting the type was a separate activity which was commonly done by the printer as the type was needed. The growth of foundries which commissioned designs of type and sold cast fouuts to printers was a later development.

The early hand moulds, about the size of a cricket ball, were simple mechanisms which most printcrs could afford. Each mould cast one size only, so a range was necded to make more than one size. To cast type, the craftsman inserted the matrix into the head of the mould and closed it; then with a ladle he poured molten typemetal (an alloy of lead with small amounts of tin, copper and antimony) into the conical opening at the bottom of the mould. He gave the mould a shake to press the metal into all the parts of the letter before hardening, and opened it up to release the cast sort, which needed only trimming to remove the excess metal before being ready for printing.

Until the industrial revolution, all type was cast in hand moulds in this way, except for large sizes which were cast in sand moulds, in type metal or brass, or cut in wood; but in the nineteenth century casting machines were developed which mechanised the process without changing it fundamentally.

The type thus cast was put into cases divided into compartments, each for a different letter. Tradition-.ally, separate cases held minuscules and capital letters, and when using them the compositor put the pairs of cases on a frame with capitals above the minuscules. This is why the two are often still called 'uppcr case' and 'lower case', even by people who have never set type by hand.

The compositor stood in front of these cases, with a composing stick in his left hand and his setting copy by him. The composing stick was a narrow tray, made or set to the length of line desired. The compositor put the letters in it in the order of his copy, with spaccs between the words. Spaces were simply pieces of metal of the same body size as the type, but lower height-to-paper; they were made with varying widths, so that at the end of the line, if the words did not exactly fill out the space available, wider or thinncr spaces could be inserted to make the line fit. This process is also called justification, but should not be confused with the justification of matrices.

When enough lines had been set in the stick, the compositor carefully lifted them out on to a larger tray called a galley. Sometimes, but not always, he inscrted strips of type metal or wood cut to thc line length, called leads, to increase the interlinear space. In this way, a page was gradually built up. In later years, when type was more plentiful and scparate proofing presses were used, the type was often proofed in long strips before being divided up into pages, and the results were known as galley proofs; but originally the pages of type went straight on the press for proofing, and correetions were made on the spot, sometimes, in the less serupulous houses, after the press run had started.

![](images/ba1099f8278c92e5f4c3265e96965a9f5850772328c426717fdf69dc282a131a.jpg)  
A printing shop in the late sixteenth century. An engraving by Philippe Galle after a drawing by Jan van der Straet, from the series Noca Reperta. The representation is comprehensive and lively rather than stricthy accurate in all details.

The first presses were built mainly of wood, with metal parts where neeessary. They were slow, and laborious to use, but they had the advantage of cheapness, ease of building and dismantling, and mobility. They were adapted from the wine press, but added to the screw mechanism a sliding track to trundle the type, with the paper laid over it, under the platen, which applied the pressure. These wooden presses, pulled by hand, remained in use for a remarkably long time, only being replaced by iron presses carly in the nineteenth eentury.

With these simple tools, printers produced some of the great books of our culture. Without the printing press, the great explosion of learning which took place during the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries would have bcen a mueh smaller affair. Texts could be multiplied quickly and eheaply as never before in the West, and moreover, they could be established, correeted and published in a way hitherto impossible, as an edition of acknowledged worth, comprising a number of copies of a book textually identical, even if decorated and bound differently. And in the way these texts were designed, there were new possibilities: lines could be moved or reset at proof stage, permitting seeond thoughts and improvements, in a way that manuseripts, for all their magnificence, did not allow.

A seribe, before he set to work to produce his one copy of a book, pouneed his vellum, sharpened his quill, and had only to decide how big his seript would be before ruling his lines and starting work. The seript itself flowed from his pen as it was needed, modelled on others, but individual, and always developing: no two a's were the same, and the writing probably changed aecording to the seribe's mood. Manuseript was always short of perfection, but it was alive. Type was quite different, in that the design of the letters was fixed before they were assembled. In the first typefaces, attempts were made to mimie the informality of manuseripts by eutting ligatures and seribal contractions, but these were slowly redueed in number, on common-sense as well as economie grounds (though never completely: we still sometimes employ the ampersand). Letters were often eut and used in several versions. But gradually the coneeption

A wooden hand-press. From the colophon of a book printed by Josse Bade (14621535), a printer first of Lyon and then Paris, who used the Latin name Ascensius.

of a fount became more definite, with twenty-four to six lower-case letters (u and j were introduced subsequently), sets of matching capitals and figurcs and, later, a related italic for contrast and emphasis, and later still a related bold, small capitals and titling, leading to the modern idea of a type family, typified by Univers, with several varieties of weight and width developed from a basic design.

Nevertheless, although the typeset page was more rigid and less vital than the manuscript, there wcre compensating advantages. Quite apart from the tcxtual benefits already referred to, there was the realisation that the forms of letters could be perfected, preserved in metal, and repeated endlessly. Thus the idea of a typeface was born.

From the earliest designs, based on contemporary manuscript hands, types quickly established a tradition of their own. Gutenberg and his heirs used only blackletter, or gothic, but with the spread of printing to Italy, especially Venice, a design of roman type was developed based on renaissance humanist script and, shortly after, a sloped and contracted version of handwriting, called italic; later still, the two were used jointly for textual articulation. They were more or less gradually adopted by the French, the Spanish, the Dutch, the English and their colonies, usually sideby-side with local forms of blackletter, and arc loosely called old face or, in the Vox classification, Garaldes, a merging of the names of the French punchcutter Claude Garamond and the Venetian printer Aldus Manutius.

![](images/b34f354812290866d0d0ce8fb8f26df1b604edd6feace50464dc6daa61d6500f.jpg)  
A pair of type cases. Although the arrangement may vary around the edges, the basic layout is constant in English-speaking countries. From Updike, 'Printing types ' (1922).

![](images/8b91048429c3278f0f25546dcaccaf31961d7f5e27b9aff2bcf267236e19c32e.jpg)

This family of derivatives from thc Venetian originals was dominant up to the middle of the eighteenth century, when a transition began towards what are equally loosely called modern faces, or Didones (a combination of the names of Didot and Bodoni), characterised by greater contrast between thins and thicks in the modelling of the letters, and vertical stress, so that the thinnest parts of an O are at twelve o'clock and six, instead of around eleven o'clock and five, as in old face types.

It was during the transition to modern face typcs that the first attempt was made to regularise type sizes. Hitherto, sizes had varied between different printers and foundries, and had been given imprecise names, often associated with the kind of work they were commonly used for, such as the French Saint-Augusti (14pt) and Cicéro (12pt), and thc English Brevier (8pt) and Long primer (10pt). The Frcnch typefounder Pierre-Simon Fournier (1712-68) introduced a system of subdivisions of the inch, called

It substituted for the traditional rough-and-ready terms such as old stylc, modern, transitional, Egyptian, sans-serif and Latin the following catcgories:

An iron hand-press. R. W. Cope's Albion was the most comnon

points, and fitted the existing named sizes into his seheme: thus the Cicéro was made the equivalent of twelve points. Later in the century the powerful typefounding and printing Didot family revised Fournier's scale, making it slightly larger, and the Didot system remains in use on the mainland of Europe, although it is not metric.

The Ameriean point system, which is also used in Britain and the rest of the English-speaking world, was not adopted until the 188os, and is based on the pica (12pt) em, which is nearly exactly a sixth of an inch, and slightly smaller than the Cicéro. When continental metal types were adapted for the American system, they were big on their bodies in the smaller sizes, and in the larger ones east on the next size of body up. Thus a 12D (Didot) type was commonly cast on 13pt, 30D on 36pt, and so on.

By the end of the first quarter of the nineteenth eentury the transition to modern face types was complete, and their development from then on was prompted more by utility than beauty; the types of the middle of the century were as bad, if not worse, than any in the four previous centuries. At the same time, the hitherto slow pace of technical development in printing began to accelerate dramatieally. Lord Stanhope's introduction of an iron hand press around 18oo was soon followed by the American Columbian designed by George Clymer, and the English Albion, from R. W. Cope. These presses used lever or knuckle meehanisms which considerably inereased the power of impression, so that a forme could be printed in one movement instead of the step-and-repeat impressions of wooden presses. Within a few years, moreover, Frederick Koenig and Andreas Bauer, two Germans working in London, had designed presses in which the paper was earried round a cylinder whieh, as it rolled over the type, applied far greater pressure than the flat platen of a hand-press could do; and Koenig and Bauer's presses were powered by steam. One was installed at The Times's printing house as early as 1814; from newspaper offices, eylinder presses spread to general printers and eventually superseded hand-presses for book work in all workshops save the private presses.

These bigger and faster machines were hungry for type, but the inventions that would fully satisfy them had to wait until the 188os. For most of the cenury, armics of hand eompositors set books and newspapers as they had always done, but on a mueh larger seale. Machines were built to cast type more quickly and reliably than hand moulds, but that type had still to be set manually. Machines were tried, such as the Kastenbein, whieh mechanised the work of the hand compositor, with the type selection governed by another important nineteenth-century invention, the keyboard; but they were slow, and a eompanion machine had to be harnessed to redistribute the type back into cases or magazines.

![](images/7b19141d1472a87a9a4403456a36ee92a943e428e63440b0a148caf9c6c384cb.jpg)

Then, in thc 188os, two American engineers, working independently, brought out machines which applied a new prineiple: instead of assembling type already cast, they assembled matrices and cast new type from them. Thus the type was always fresh, and laborious distribution after printing was unnecessary since the type went straight back into the melting pot. The Linotype, invented by the German immigrant Ottmar Mergenthaler in Baltimore and first installed at the Nem York Tribune office in 1886, earried a magazine of matriees whieh were released by the aetion of the keyboard to fall into a line; the operator the s he e   ld sug  ieTe slugs were easy to handle, but less easy to correet, and Linotypes were more commonly used for setting newspapers than books. Tolbert Lanston's Monotype maehines, developed in Washington at the same time,

The Monotype, as its namc implies, cast scparate sorts, and so it could be uscd for both text sctting and for filling cases for hand-sctting; and corrcctions were simpler than with Linotypc slugs.

Both machincs, but particularly the Linotype, necdcd a grcat number of matrices. Before they appearcd, the traditional hand-cut punches had kept up with the demand; but, though strong, they were not indestructable and, as we havc seen, thcy could not be preciscly duplicated. Coincidentally and fortunately, thc necessary invention was madc at the same time as Lanston's and Mergcnthaler's. In 1884 the Milwaukce typc founder Linn Boyd Benton (1844-1932) patented a punch-cutting machine which worked from a large pattcrn: by means of a pantograph, the minute cngraving drill was moved around the outlinc of thc lctter on the end of the punch.

Thc Benton machinc had far-reaching implications for the dcsign of type. Thc first was the relative casc of the new technique. There had bcen cascs alrcady of dcsigncrs who wcrc not punchcutters conveying their idcas to craftsmen by mcans of words or drawings: Baskcrvillc, Bodoni and Bulmer spring to mind, and therc was the wcll documcnted casc of the pionecring design of the Frcnch Romain du roi, conccived at the turn of the scventeenth and eightecnth ccnturies by the Acadcmie Française according to precisc mathcmatical rulcs, thc drawings meticulously cngraved on a grid, and the punchcs cut by Philippe Grandjcan. But in gencral, the scarcity of skilled intcrpretative punchcutters severely limited these cndeavours. Now it was possible for anyone who could draw letters, and even people who could not, to dcsign typcfaces which could be lickcd into practical shape by the foundry drawing office.

The sccond implication was less bcncficial, and concerned the question of scale. The hand punchcutter necessarily worked at actual scale, and made minute adjustments to thc shapes of letters accord-

The pantographic machine, however, could cut many sizes from one set of drawings, and the instinctive modifications of the craftsmen wcre endangered. The more scrupulous manufacturers cut fewer sizes from any one set of drawings, and made more sets of drawings with the adaptions that were needcd, but their example was not always followed. With the introduction of filmsetting, dcscribed in a later chapter, type sizes were altered with interchangeable lenses, and types conceived for filmsetting had to be designed with the knowlcdge that one set of drawings would have to work properly in all composition sizes. Before the introduction of the Benton machine, types had been preservcd as sets of punches, one set for each size, cach slightly different. Now one set of master drawings served the samc purpose.

Thus at the beginning of the twentieth century, type designers faced great challenges. Because punches could be cut by machine from drawings, and did not necessarily need the interpretative skills of a hand punchcutter, it was easier for a non-specialist to design type. This sometimes fostered talent previously dcbarred from the field by lack of technical expertise, and gave opportunities to the amateur designer. An cxample is the Cheltenham face, which was designed in 1896 by an American architcct, Bertram Goodhue, for the private use of the Cheltenham Press of New York. It was cut for them by American Type Founders, for hand setting. Although it has found few friends among typographical critics, it proved so popular that ATF and Linotype in partnership issued it to the printing trade, and between 1904 and 1911 ATF's immmensely skilled and prolific designer Morris Benton (1872-1948), the son of the inventor of the pantographic machine, drew eighteen different weights and other variants.

A handsomer portent of things to come was thc appearance of entury in 1895, the year before Cheltenham. It was a strengthened modern face designed by Theodore L. DeVinne (1828-1914), the distinguished American master-printer, for The Century, a magazine which was produced at his works; and again it was cut by ATF, who afterwards marketed it. Morris Benton produced eighteen variants between 1900 and 1928, including the ever popular Century Schoolbook.

The manufacturers of the new typcsetting machines, Monotype and Linotype, took several decades to start commissioning new designs, and at first remained contcnt to adapt existing faccs for their systems. But before the old century was out they took the stcp of licensing the rights to manufacture their machinery in other countries; Linotype formed a British company in 1895, and Monotype in 1897. Thesc companics were autonomous, and their typographical programmes quite separate from the American parents'. As we shall see, the team of engincers at English Monotype's Salfords works, and the typographical guidancc of Stanley Morison, led the Corporation to play a significant role in fostering the revival of printing between thc two world wars, from which it also bencfited grcatly, but all the companics appointed advisers and encouraged their own designers.

Also in the 189os, there took place in Britain an experiment in printing which, although rootcd in the past in almost all respects, had a powerful if indirect influcnce on the designers of the next century. The pioneer industrial designer and socialist William Morris (1834-96), in thc last years of his life, returned to one of his earliest ambitions, and sct up a private press, called the Kelmscott Press. Books were printed there using hand presses, on dampened hand-made paper, in hand-set type designed by Morris himself and cut by a hand punchcutter, with wood-engraved ornament. The typographic style was backward looking, and recalled Morris's much loved incunabula, those books printed before 1500. His greatest book, the Kelmscott Chaucer, is a singular and unrivalled achievement. Yet despite this, Morris's search for excellent materials, for harmony between type and illustrations on the printed page, and in particular his discriminating investigation of early printed books for models for his own types, all commended thcmselves to the next generation, and many typographers, while in no way copying Morris's style, were profoundly stimulated by his ideas and his books.

Morris designed two types for his Press. The second, cut in two sizes called Troy and Chaucer, was a round blacklettcr of the kind known as rotunda. The first, Golden (1891), was more influential, because based on the magnificent roman types produced by Nicolas Jenson of Venice in the early 147os (though the immediate model, which Morris followed by inking in letters on photographic enlargements, was printed from Jenson types by Jacobus Rubeus in 1476). In drawing over the letters, Morris made thcm much heavier. It is usual to say that he made them more 'gothic', but just as accurate to point out that he also made them more Victorian, strengthcning the 'Egyptian' display faces.

![](images/e73ae78ba4ab68b522d8ac5b519e6f5c391044392aaa0833cf0a04ef2c38c367.jpg)  
William Morris's Golden type (1891). Morris also designed the border.

Morris's choice of the Jenson model was widely imitated, and led to a flood of 'Venetians' and 'Jensons'. Its immediate successor was a far more elegant version for the Doves Press, begun in 1900 by the barrister-turned-bookbinder-turned-printer Thomas J. Cobden-Sandcrson (1840-1922). The

Doves Bindery had bound books for the Kelmscott Press, but the Doves Press, though it shared with Kelmscott the Jenson model for its one and only type, its hand-prcss technology, and its adviser in the person of Emery Walker (1851-1933), the éminence grise of the English private press movement, produccd very different books. They were spare and undecorated, and in keeping with this the type was made lean and austcre. The Doves Bible is another of the great achievements of the private presses, and it is hard to belicve that only nine years separate it from the Chaucer. Whereas the Kelmscott book looked medieval, the Doves one belongs to the ncw age.

In the subsequent bitter arguments between Emery Walker and Cobden-Sanderson over thc management of the Press, which culminated in 1916 when Cobden-Sanderson threw the type into the Thames off Hammersmith Bridge to prevent its usc by anyone else, both partics claimed authorship of the type; but it seems clear that though Cobden-Sanderson's ideals may have influcnced the way it was refined, Walker's technical knowledge and the skill of his draughtsman Percy Tiffin were essential to its success. Walker closely supervised the punchcutter Edward Prince, who had previously engraved the Kelmscott types, similary superviscd, and went on to produce the types of sevcral of the other English private prcsses, as well as those of the Cranach Press, begun in 1913 by thc German diplomat and art patron Count Harry Kessler (1868-1938) at Weimar. The Ashcndcne, Eragny and Vale Presscs all commissioned types which were at best individual, and at the worst a hideous reversion to nineteenth-ccntury archaism, but the Cranach Press returncd to the classical disciplinc of Doves. Kessler was a cosmopolitan who had becn to school in England, and came to London for the advicc he needed for his Press. A roman very similar to thc Dovcs type was produced by Walker, Tiffin and Prince, but the same team ran into difficulties with a companion italic lower casc, which neither the Doves type nor Jenson had.

The problems they encountered, which have been well chronicled by John Dreyfus,\* wcre met with again and again in one form or another whcnever

manufacturers tried to reconstruct historic faces, and so are worth summarising here.

The italic model chosen by Walker was a quirky and irregular type designed by the Venetian scribe Tagliente about fifty years after Jenson. The page of Tagliente's 1525 writing manual which Walker enlarged was badly printed, which made it difficult for Tiffin, who did not know very much about type, to deduce their shape suceessfully when inking in the letters. In all such eases, when an original type no longer exists in metal but only in the form of printed pages, the draftsman must try to perceive the outline of the characters as if they had been cleanly inkcd and well printed on smooth paper, eliminating all the splodges produced in the originals by ink spread and impression on rough paper. He must then bear in mind that the first punchcutter deliberately engraved his letters on the fine side, knowing that they would be made bolder in the printing. Only then can he decide how far to restore the accidentals to prevent the type from looking too deodorised when printed with modern presses on smooth papers. All kinds of ethical considerations about the nature and purpose of workmanship arise, which were argued over in the decades that followed.

Prince cut a set of punches from Tiffin's drawings and made smoke-proofs, which were trial impressions made by coating the punches with soot from a candle flame and pressing them on paper. Kessler saw how the type had been misinterpreted and, aware that he was engaged in recutting the first chancery italic in modern times, asked the calligrapher Edward Johnston (1872-1944) to supply Prince with new drawings. Johnston, who held firm Art and Crafts principles, felt that he should only do 'eharacter sketches' to guide Prince, who was the craftsman and therefore in control. Prince, however, was a craftsman of a different school, who was used to working with more precise instructions (and in this, although he was a hand punchcutter, he was reacting as any drawing office would). It took all Kessler's diplomacy to persuade Johnston to provide finished drawings, and even then the type was only half successful. Johnston's character comes through too forcefully for the type to be considered a true historical recutting, and yet the constraints of following Tagliente's type clearly made him unhappy. Some designers resolved this conflict, as we shall see in later chapters, and created new types from old models, but Johnston's calligraphic genius was hampered by it. It is ironical that his best-known type, designed two years later in 1915, was a sans-serif, commissioned by Frank Pick of London Transport for use in the Underground.

The work of the Cranach Press was interrupted by World War I, and then by Kessler's political career, and it was not until 193 1 that an edition of Rilke was printed in the Johnston italic. In 1933, with Hitler's rise to power, Kessler left Germany and the Press closed down.

# Old types for new machines

Thc introduction of mechanical typesetting at the end of the ninetecnth century seemed to many to signal the cnd of the established foundries, who supplicd the printing trade with cast type for hand setting. In the event, the new machines took longer to be completely accepted, and many foundries survived longer than anyone could havc predicted.

They did so in a varicty of ways. Firstly, because among the mechanical systems only thc Monotype could, using the Supcrcaster (introduced in 1928), cast type in display sizes for hand setting up to 72 pt (that is a body height of six pica ems, or one inch, the largest size in which mctal type is normally cast). For their display sizes the uscrs of slug casters such as thc Linotype had to use either a display slug casting machine such as the Ludlow, which had a different repertoire of designs, or hand-sct type from a Monotype machine or from a foundry.

Also, some of the more astutc foundries rapidly adapted thcmselves to the new conditions by amalgamating with other foundries, their formcr rivals, the better to resist the new threat to their livelihoods, and by diversification into printing machine manufacturc. Some also formed links with the opposition. The great German foundry of D. Stempel\*\* in Frankfurt, founded in 1895, absorbed a number of smaller firms over the years, sometimcs in partnership with their closest rivals in Germany, the Baucr and Berthold foundries, and in 1918 acquired a controlling intercst in the small but entcrprising Klingspor foundry in Offenbach. But Stempel also, as early as 190o, signed an agreement with the Berlin branch of Linotype to manufacture their matrices, and the close collaboration ovcr many years bctween Stempel and Linotypc in the developmcnt of new typefaces remains a model of productive harmony.

On the other side of the Atlantic, the United States, as the cradlc of the new machines, naturally felt their impact even earlier.

Up to thc last dccade of the ninetecnth ccntury, most of the type foundries there had depended for the bulk of their salcs on supplying newspapers with small sizes of type for tcxt setting by hand, and had found this market killed almost overnight by thc Linotype machinc. The newspapers, whilc thcy were still using foundry type, had always wantcd it quickly to keep production going, and in this had encouraged small foundries in almost evcry city that had a newspaper, dependant on it and so extremely vulnerable. In 1892, of the twenty-nine foundries which were to form American Type Founders, only a handful were in a strong position. In that year twenty-five of the foundries decided to join together to form ATF, and the other four joined latcr. Thirtecn of the weakest quickly closed down, and the federation that rcmained lacked cohesion and a sense of direction until thc appointment in 1894 of Robcrt Nclson\* (1851- 1926) as general manager, and in 1901 as president. Nclson realised that the various constitucnt foundries were run by talented men who were undcr-employed becausc of the group's policy of kecping each foundry separate. Joseph W. Phinncy of the Dickinson Foundry in Boston had tried to issue Morris's Golden type commercially and, on Morris's refusal, had produced his own version, Jcnson Old Style, in 1893. He was a man of considcrable perccption, and in 1896 issued the first type scnt to him from Chicago by a young aspiring designer called Frederic Goudy, with results which will bc described later. Linn Boyd Benton had invented the pantographic punchcutting machine which had becn so helpful to thc composing machine manufacturers who wcre now threatening

Morris Benton drew many competent vcrsions of classic types, notably Cloistcr and Garamond; he skilfully expanded many faces into families; and he produced a variant of Goudy Old Style, called Goudy Catalogue, which is arguably better than the original.

Nelson managed to insist that thesc independently minded men work for ATF rather than for themselvcs. He had the foresight to see that while from then on text would almost always be set by machine, displayed matter needed foundry type, and with the growth of advcrtising and thc cmergence of the advertising designer therc would bc an increasing hunger for ncw designs. Nclson was always a type man, even when ATF diversified into printing machinery; he developed the idea of thc type family, in which a design could be produced in bold, semibold, extra bold, and expanded and condensed forms without losing its character, and unity could be given to a typographic design when employing different wcights of type. He also backcd, in spite of the reluetance of his colleagues, ATF's first great success, the cutting of the Cheltcnham family, in association with Mergenthaler Linotype.

Nelson was assisted at ATF by his advertising manager Henry Lewis Bullen (1857-1938), who was a far morc influential figure than his position might suggest. He had been with thc company from the start, but it was after he rejoined it in 19o8 aftcr a period clsewhcre that he began to build up the great typographical refercnce library for which hc is best known, and from whose resources he and Morris Benton drew in devising thcir variations on the Jenson model sanctificd by William Morris, Venetian (191 1) and the highly popular Cloister (1913).

In 1914, Bullen, taking his lead from thc Frcnch foundry Deberny & Peignot, suggested a new historical model for recutting, the 'Garamond' types used by the Imprimerie Nationale in Paris, and though production of the ATF version was held up until 19 17 as the foundries had been requisitioncd for war work, it was to prove widely influential.

By comparison with thc more adventurous of the European foundries, who pursued an cnergetic programme of persuading outside designers to create innovative typefaces, ATF's more cautious policy, with onc or two exceptions such as Goudy Old Style,

Their conservatism was equalled, even exceeded, by that of the manufacturers of the new typesetting machines, whose engineering innovations were not matched by any corresponding audacity in design for many years. We must remember that Monotype and Linotype were not selling type design, or even type, but composing machines, and the temptation was understandably to equip them with faces that looked as like the foundry type they were replacing as possible. The frst English Monotype faces were simply recuttings of the old styles and moderns then familiar to printers, publishers and readers, and the Corporation considered adding to the repertoire only if a customer made it a condition of buying a new machine, or otherwise brought influence to bear.

In 1915, for instance, William Maxwell of the Edinburgh printers R. & R. Clark, who printed the plays of George Bernard Shaw, came to Monotype with a problcm. Shaw was type-conscious, and insisted on his writings being set in Caslon, the perennial English favourite old face dating from the early eighteenth century; and moreover on foundry

Monotype Caslon (1915).

Caslon, hand-set. Maxwell wanted to change to Monotype composition for reasons of cost, but Monotype had not issued Caslon. However, they agrced to cut a trial version, and Maxwell presented Shaw with two specimen pages, one set on the Monotype and one by hand, without telling him which was which. Shaw preferred the machine set page, and the day was carried.

The English Monotype Corporation\* at this time, although its typographical achievement, with three exceptions, had been unspectacular, was technically proficient and well managed. The two chief officers were both American. The managing director, Harold Duncan, was a man of cducation and vision, and the works manager, Frank Pierpont, was a highly skilled engineer, who ruled thc works at Salfords, just south of London, like a separate empire. He was assisted in the drawing office by Fritz Steltzer, whom hc had brought from Germany. Steltzer was responsible for the redrawing and adaptation of most of the great

Thc work of the drawing office in all type manufacture using pantographic engraving consisted of preparing large patterns for the pantograph to follow, and in the case of mechanical composition each machine had constraints which the draftsmen had to bear in mind. The particular limits of the Monotypc machine lay in the makc-up of its diecase. Thc matrices were arrangcd in grid formation in the case, with all thc lctters of a given width in a row. The letters in any one row had to bc of equal width. Inevitably, thc drawing office had to do some expanding and contracting of letters to makc them fit, and their skill lay in making such distortion as unnoticeable as possible. Double and singlc f-ligatures were particularly uscful sorts for this purpose, since thc spacc bctween the letters could bc altercd without changing the lettcrs thcmselvcs.

Such skilful adaptations of lctter forms to fit the diecase were accepted by most designers, with the vocal cxception of the Dutchman Jan van Krimpen. But others, notably Eric Gill, pointed out the tendency of the drawing office to rcgularisc letters with too much use of rulers, compasses, and French curves. The subtle concave waisting of uprights, the organic growth of serifs from stems, and the flow of freehand drawing, were oftcn lost in rigid mechanical rcdrawing.

Nevcrtheless, objections such as these were minor criticisms of Monotype's success: in the first decades of thc ccntury thcrc was less to criticise because less was being done. There were, however, threc types which were a foretaste of things to come. These were Imprint, Plantin and Bodoni.

The Imprint was a printing trade journal which appearcd in 1913. It was a crusading venture, which attemptcd to raisc printers' consciousness of design. Not altogether surprisingly for the time, it did not last a year, but its contcnts wcre remarkably informative and wide-ranging, dealing with aesthetics as well as such more practical matters as printing processes, trade education and costing. The editorial board reflected the range of interest, including as it did the calligrapher Edward Johnston as well as Gerard Meynell, manager of the Westminster Press, who printed the journal.

Imprint Old Face, Series 101, was thc first Monotype face of any distinction, and their twclfth most commercially successful; and it is still in common usc today. From the first The Imprint's editors made it clear that their type was for general sale, as part of thcir declared ambition to raise standards in the tradc. This is often cited as evidencc of their altruism, although they undoubtedly took the practical view that widespread salcs would help the magazine's parlous finances, by defraying the costs of development and cutting.

Monotype's other early success has proved even more cnduring. Plantin, Scries I Io, was proposed by Pierpont and Steltzer at Salfords, and cut in 1913, only eight months aftcr Imprint. It was designed with the specific intent of building the effect of ink-sprcad into a typeface intended for printing on smooth and coatcd papers, whieh in effect mcant that it was a bolder type than its model. As wc have secn, the early punehcutters reduccd the wcight of their letters, knowing that the printing proccss would make them bolder. The model for Plantin was a type used in an Index characterum printed in 1905, listing the types used by the Plantin-Moretus Press in Antwverp, and compiled by Max Rooscs, the first director of the Plantin-Morctus Museum, the fine collection of early printing presses and equipment still housed in the original buildings. The complete fount, the work of Robert Granjon, Garamond's approximate eontemporary, was never used by the grcat Christophe

Plantin (1514-89), although he used somc letters from it to augment a Garamond fount he had; but it was bought by the Moretus family, his hcirs, later in the seventeenth century. Over the years, some wrong fount letters wcre introduced by the Moretuses, retained in the Index, and reproduced in Monotype Plantin.

The choice of the Plantin model was adventurous in that it was not a fifteenth-century Venetian ype, but a sixtecnth-eentury Freneh one. In this, Pierpont was making a very similar choice to Bullen's when he selected 'Garamond' for recutting at much the same time. But Pierpont simply wanted a type that was large on its body and economical of space, seleeted the Moretus Granjon for that praetical reason, and had it much redrawn. ATF Garamond, whieh began to appear at the end of World War I and was bought eagerly on both sides of the Atlantic, stayed much eloser to its original, whieh was chosen more for its aesthetic merits.

This original was a colleetion of types at the Imprimerie Nationale in Paris, called the caractères de I'nuiversité. They became widely known by being used to set the introduction of Anatole Claudin's Histoire de l'imprimerie en France au XVe et au XVI siècle, published between 1900 and 1914 in four magnificent volumes, and were aseribed by Claudin and Arthur Christian, direetor of the Imprimerie Nationale, to Garamond. The authority of these men made the attribution generally accepted, and though Bullen had doubts that the type aetually was by Garamond, he did not broadcast them. It was not until 1926 that Bullen's former assistant Beatricc Warde, writing under her pen-name Paul Beaujon in The Fleuron 5, revealed that the caractères de I'université were copies of Garamond's types cut in thc early seventeenth century by the little known Jean Jannon of Sedan.

The suecess of ATF Garamond, however, was untroubled by such questions, and the type quiekly began to attract imitators. Shortly after his appointment as 'art adviser' to the Ameriean Lanston Monotype Company in 1920, Frederie Goudy suggested to its president J. Maury Dove 'that there secmed to be a movement on the part of foundries to revive some of the old book types and "why should not the Monotype present its products first instead of following the others?" Though this rather brash approach did less than justice to ATF, Dove was persuaded to try the idea out. Goudy took his copy of Claudin, and drew his own version, which was considerably closer to its original than most of his types tended to be to their declared models, though it was, by comparison with the carlier ATF version and the slightly later English Monotype one, somewhat gauche in its details. Goudy had a certain amount of trouble with the Philadelphia drawing office, who tried to regularisc the irregularities of his drawings, and he had to appeal to Dove to have them followed exactly. The type appeared in 192 1 to much aeclaim, and was only overshadowed by the English Monotype version. Goudy, writing in 1946 in A half-century of type desigu, appeared unaware that Garamond's authorship of the face had been disproved, and only noted that his spelling Garamont had been questioned on many oecasions.

The relationship betwcen the two Monotype versions puzzled many people, including Daniel Berkeley Updike, the great Boston printer, although his curiosity was academie: he was a slow and reluetant convert to meehanieal composition and did not allow it at his Merrymount Press until 1932. He wrote\* to Stanley Morison of English Monotype in 1923 asking for cnlightenment, and observing that a trade magazine in the United States had referred to the English cutting as 'a version of the Goudy type'. Morison replied with somc heat, objecting to Goudy's (or his press agent's) tacking his own name on to Garamond's, and making it clear that the types were separately conceived. He defended the English cutting, particularly its better background research, and concluded, 'I am sure that the English Monotype version is not in every respect to be commended. At least however it is known as Monotype-Garamond. I shudder to think of the terrible things that Mr Goudy now threatens to do. I suppose he too will do a Blado italie  and eall it Goudy  I must get ahcad first.' Morison did. He and Updike often reviled Goudy in their correspondenec, though Morison was polite enough about him in print.

mercly the result of an attempt to reproduce as nearly as possible the form and spirit of the "Garamond" letter. I made no attempt to eliminate the mannerisms or deficiencies of his famous type, realizing that they came not by intention, but rather through the punch-cutters's handling, to his lack of tools of prevision and his crude materials; for he worked "by eye" and not by rule.

'I did find it impossible to eliminate, in my own rendition of the letter, that subtle something we call "personality", that something made up of items so intangible as practically to be imperceptible when individual types are compared, yet clearly manifest when the page they form is viewed as a whole. The subtleties of "Garamond's" drawing I couldn't neglect, yet I did not consciously include them in my own drawings, and these are the touches that mark my face as belonging to the prcscnt and not to the sixteenth century.'

Meanwhile, in England, Harold Dunean at Monotype was under pressure to eut a Garamond, in partieular from the firm of Lund Humphries, who wanted it for the 1922 edition of their printing trade publieation The Penrose annual. In the January-February 1922 issue of The Monotype recorder, the handsome house journal of the English company, the type was announeed, and work was begun. Series

156 was issued towards the end of the year, and was to prove Monotype's eighth most suecessful faee.

One of the figures behind the English Monotype Garamond was a young typographer and printing historian ealled Stanley Morison, then aged thirtythree. Although Morison's work as a designer is deseribed in a later section, as an adviser his work is inextricably linked with Monotype. Almost entirely self-taught, he had made himself an expert on typographical history, but he had a practical turn of mind which saw in the past a wealth of material useful for the present, and a persuasive pen when it came to convineing his employers that they should baek his judgement (although he always maintained that they did not employ him, but he them).

The issue of The Monotype recorder announeing Garamond was printed at the Cloister Press, where Morison was then working, and the announcement was set in ATF Garamond, bought at his insistenee. It also had two artieles he had written. Moreover, he had been asked by Erie Humphries to design The Penrose annual, and probably helped by that route to influenee Monotype to undertake the eutting. And he began his direet association with Monotype by supplementing Claudin's book with extra material from the Imprimerie Nationale for the drawing officc to work from. Early in 1923 his position as typographical adviser was made more formal, and though he had a long struggle with Pierpont, who understandably felt that his hegemony was threatencd by an amateur, his influence over the Corporation's policy was incalculable.

Even after Monotype Garamond was cut and issued, Morison did not losc interest in its origins. Beatricc Wardc had moved to London, and latcr became an imaginative publicity manager at Monotype. Morison cncouragcd hcr to investigate Bullen's doubts about Garamond's authorship of the type, which resultcd in her 1926 article on Jean Jannon already mentioned. In a postscript, she noted two other recuttings which used as models not the caractères de l'université, but the genuine Garamond types shown in a 1592 spccimen sheet from the Egenolff-Berner foundry in Frankfurt, who had obtained matrices aftcr Garamond's death. The first was the Granjon type cut in 1924 by English Linotype to thc designs of Georgc W. Jones, which was, in spite of its namc, 'immeasurably the best of the modern revivals of this letter'. The other 'Egenolff' Garamond was cut by the Stempel foundry in 1924, and was copied by Mergenthaler Linotype in what Beatrice Warde called 'a poor variant'. This is the version currcntly available from Linotype in digital form.

Warde pursued her altruism in summing up the qualities of the Jannon type which was the model for Monotype Garamond as having 'a certain "prickle" to cut the eye, the italic a certain erratic twist, that justify our calling it a somewhat precious design, one which should have been left in the romantic privacy of the Imprimerie Nationale.' In this, she was harsher than Morison, who defended the type, and prefcrred to call the prickle 'crispness'.

The next type cut by Monotype was Baskerville, begun in January 1923, again following thc lead of ATF. Interest in the work of the great eighteenthcentury English type designer had been rekindled by Straus and Dent's biography of 19o7, and Stephenson Blake, thc Shefficld foundry, had revived their vcrsion of the Baskerville copies produced by the Fry foundry after Baskerville's death. It was these copies that ATF used in their 1917 cutting, working from strikes provided by Stephenson Blake. Monotype, at Morison's suggestion, decidcd to return to thc original, and used as a model an edition of Terence's comedies printcd by Baskerville in 1772. Although

Almost at the same time as Baskcrville, Monotypc produccd a far more precise faesimilc. It was called Poliphilus aftcr its modcl, the type cut by Francesco Griffo for Aldus Manutius of Venice for the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili (1 499), one of thc most famous of illustrated incunabula. The Mcdici Society of London wcre planning a translation of thc book with reproductions of the woodcuts, and wanted an exact copy of thc type to set it in. Thc Monotype works duly obliged. Morison, in writing about thc type in thc Tally in 1953, cxpresscd dissatisfaetion with the rcsult only because he had been too busy clscwhcrc to seareh out the elearcst imprcssions of the original for them to work from. He did not appear to rcgret, as hc might well have done, thc illogicality of rcproducing in a type the furry outlincs of the original which were due to the accidcntals of ink-spread in one partieular ease, although he later qualified his position in a letter to Van Krimpen, crcditing Pierpont with the decision. He was involved in the type chiefly in finding a companion italic, which thc I 499 Aldine cdition did not have. This he found in the chaneery italics based on thc designs of the scribe Lodovieo dcgli Arrighi and uscd by thc Papal printer Antonio Blado of Rome early in the sixtecnth century; and as if to emphasisc the diffcrcnt backgrounds of thc two types, thc italic was eonfusingly given a scparate name, Blado. In a later ease where a roman based on a Venctian original was yokcd to a chancery italic, thc same praetiee was followed, and Centaur roman has an italic callcd Arrighi.

Thc first of Morison's folio historical works, Four centuries of fine printing, was publishcd by Ernest Benn in 1924, sct in Poliphilus-and-Blado, and printed at the Cambridgc Univcrsity Press. The new University Printcr, Walter Lewis, had been works managcr at thc Cloistcr Press when Morison was designer thcre, and had formcd a high opinion of him, which was not universally shared at first by other master printers. He was able to persuade the Syndics, thc governing body of the University Press, to appoint Morison as typographic adviser to the Press and, from 1925 on, Morison was able to create a demand for Monotype faces which greatly helped his plans at the Corporation.

In 1924, Monotype began the cutting of their next major revival, and the first in which Morison can be said to have been fully responsible for both the roman and italic, in that no other foundry had anticipated him, and no outside customer had asked for the type to be cut. This typc, Fournier, Series 185, grew out of his admiration for the types of the French designer Pierre-Simon Fournier, an enthusiasm that was shared by his friend and mentor Updike (whom he was to meet the same year after a voluminous correspondence) and expressed in the latter's influential two-volume study, Printing types, their history, forms and use, which had appearcd in 1922.

By the autumn of 1924 the Monotypc works had cut two trial weights in 14pt of the Fournier model, and needed a decision on which one to proceed with. But Morison was away in the United States, and in October Harold Duncan died, being replaced by his second-in-command, William Burch. In the confusion of the ehange, the lighter weight of letter was cut. Morison bitterly regretted this decision and, surely rightly, always preferred the hcavier version; he managed to rescue this in the one size that was cut, ealled it Barbou, and used it for the last three numbers of his journal The Fleuron. In the 1950s some smaller sizes were cut, but too late for the type to achicve thc popularity it deservcd. Morison's disappointment over this mistaken cutting coloured his relations with Monotype for some years, while on the other side Burch took a little time to show the same support for his advice that Duncan had donc. As part of his historical studies, but also in order to gain cnough technical knowlcdge to out-manocuvre thc oftcn obstructive Monotype works, Morison began to work with some hand punchcutters, a fast disappcaring species of craftsman. He first became intercsted in thc cutting by Georges Plumet of the foundry version of thc Arrighi italic designed by Frcdcric Warde, Beatricc's husband, and then pursued his interest with the trials of Eric Gill's Perpetua.

Around the bcginning of 1926 Morison began a correspondence with the great Dutch typc designcr Jan van Krimpen, whosc first type, Lutetia, had appcared in its foundry version the year before. Van Krimpen's review of The Fleurou in the Dutch journal

Het Boek set it going, but the exchange of letters soon branched out into other subjects, and continued until Van Krimpen's death in 1958, interrupted only by World War II. Both men held pronounced opinions, and their correspondence is a curious blend of the crusty and the affectionate. Very early on, Van Krimpen taxed Morison and Monotype with being too concerned with recuttings of types of the past, and neglecting new designers. It was a theme he returned to repcatedly, although Monotype cut three of his four major book faces. Morison wrote to him in 1926, within a few months of his first letter:

'You are quite right to object to the copying of old types which is a current characteristic of English typography. It is found at present impossible to secure a new design of any interest. On the other hand, it seems a pity that a number of fine classical types are out of reach of printers of today because they have been destroyed by careless printers of the past. I think, however, that in two or three years it will be possible that the printing community in England will have been educated to the point of preferring a new work of art rather than a reproduction, and it may then be possible to persuade type founders to employ the services of an artist.'

His acquiescent tone was undoubtedly promptcd by his having already begun to persuade Eric Gill to design Perpetua. Although Perpetua and the enormously successful Gill Sans were plainly Gill's designs, without Morison's activity as both impresario and stage manager they would never have appearcd, and they, together with his work in promoting the designs of Van Krimpen, and the production of Times Ncw Roman, answer the criticism that Morison was concerned only with old types.

In January 1928, howcvcr, he returned to the practical championship of thc romans cut for Aldus Manutius, and instigated his most successful revival, cut by Monotype as Scrics 270 and called Bembo, after the writcr of thc modcl used, Cardinal Bembo's short treatise De Aetna (1495). Morison always defended the Aldine romans as distinct from thc earlier Venetian faccs of Jenson and his followers, in reaction to the prcferences of Morris and Emery Walker, and we must bear in mind that his was the pioneering work. In thc Tally he wrote:

'In the pages of De Aetna the type, then new, looks almost fresh as if it had come off a present-day type caster. Manifestly it did come from the hand of a uniquely competent engraver who, perhaps because Aldus had access

The lower casc is more brilliant than any of its predecessors, and the design as a whole ranks with the roman of Louis XIV (which brought to a close the two- hundred year reign of the Aetna type) as one of the major typographical "watersheds".'

The roman was cut to everyone's satisfaction by the end of 1928, but once again there was the problem of a companion italic which did not exist in thc Aldine original. Morison had takcn the unusual stcp of commissioning a modern calligrapher, Alfred Fairbank, to design an italic based looscly on an Arrighi original. Morison always prcferred this type, which was later called Fairbank or Narrow Bembo, but at Monotype there was opposition to it as being too condenscd and jittery, and it was dropped in favour of a blander and more harmonious italie derived from a design by the Venctian scribe, Tagliente, contemporary with Arrighi. Bembo was issued early in 1929, and has been highly regarded ever sinee, ranking eleventh out of all the Monotype faces.

The production of Bembo was almost exactly concurrent with the Monotype adaptation of Bruce Rogers's Centaur, which was initiated and supervised by Rogers himself with no detectable help from Morison. Indeed, in the Tally he wrote, 'its rccutting for Monotype composition necessarily, as it was private property, lay outside the programme adopted by the Corporation in 1922.'

The programme referred to was one 'of typographical design, rational, systematic and corresponding effectivcly with the foresceable necds of printing', which Morison wrotc that he had laid beforc Harold Duncan in 1922, which was accepted by Duncan and ratified by his successor Burch. Doubts have been cast on this claim by James Moran.\* Nevertheless, there is evidence that from early on Morison had ambitions for Monotype that were wide in scopc if unclear in all the details, though he probably had the sense to reveal them to the Corporation gradually, as both his prestige and the perceptible needs of the trade devcloped.

In 1929 Morison bcgan his association with The Times, whose history he wrote, whose Literary Supplement hc edited, and whose appearance hc trans-
