formed. But in spite of the prodigious labour involved in the design and eutting of The Times type, he found time to push through a projeet very elose to his heart, the reinstatement of the fame of the newspaper owner, publisher and type founder John Bell (1745 1831), whose types he had admired for many years.

At the Bibliothèque Nationale, at the beginning of 1925, he had eome aeross a prospeetus issued by Bell's British Library in 1788, set in a type whieh he reeognised as the same as one used by Updike at the Merrymount Press, ealled Mountjoye, and by Bruee Rogers at the Riverside Press in Boston, where it was ealled Brimmer. Brimmer had been bought as type in 1864 by Henry O. Houghton, of the publishers Houghton Mifflin, who owned the Riverside Press. Updike in 1903 bought strikes from Houghton's suppliers, Stephenson Blake of Sheffield, who eould not, however, tell him where the type eame from. Morison's diseovery at the Bibliothèque Nationale led to his researches into Bell's eareer whieh resulted in a book, John Bell, printed and published by the Cambridge University Press in 1930, where it was hand-set in type newly east by Stephenson Blake from the original matriees.

The book's sueeess, and an exhibition of Bell's publications put on to eclebrate the centenary of his death the following year, persuaded Burch to recut Bell for Monotype eomposition, and Stephenson Blake gave their permission. With the pressure of work on Times, nothing more was done until the end of November 193 1, when the Monotype works was told to go ahead.

Morison eonsidered Bell's type the first modern faee and, while influeneed by Freneh types of the period, patriotieally and deliberately British. Bell's puneheutter Riehard Austin eut a sharper letter than any hitherto attained, and twenty years later moved to Edinburgh to cut the so-ealled Seoteh romans whieh had sueh an influenee on type design in the nineteenth eentury. Morison was unusually satisfied with the Monotype eutting, ealling it "an admirable reproduetion.'

There was a gap of five years before the last major type revival Morison undertook, Ehrhardt, Series 453, whieh was one of the most useful. Ehrhardt eame out in 1937, a few months after the death of Frank Pierpont, to whom, in spite of their differences, Morison's programme owed so mueh. Work on The Times kept Morison busy, but by 1936, with the first volume of his History out, he was in a position to give more time to a fifteen-year-old enthusiasm. This was for a seventeenth-eentury type, thought to have been of Duteh origin, and resembling the Oxford University Press's Fell types (another of his passions); it was cast by the Stempel foundry of Frankfurt from materials formerly belonging to Drugulin of Leipzig, and known as Janson. In one of his earliest letters to Updike, in 1921, Morison asked if he knew of the type, and Updike revealed that he had had some at Merrymount sinee 1903, having bought it from Drugulin. Morison eontinued to worry away at the type's history, and through the printing historian Gustav Mori (1872-1950) loeated a 1710 speeimen of the type from the Ehrhardt foundry in Frankfurt. (It was not until the 195os that the typographieal scholars Harry Carter and George Buday revealed that it was riginally the work of Hungarin puneheutter Nieolas Kis.)

In 1936, when both Mergenthaler Linotype (1933) and Lanston Monotype (1935) had issued recuttings of Janson, English Monotype were under pressure to do the same, and had eut a trial size. Morison found it too heavy and rotund, and issued new instructions for the drawing offee in January 1937 for a type made larger on its body and more condensed. As a result,

# ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyzfiflfffiffææ ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyzfiffflææ

Ehrhardt is an economieal facc, legible, and with enough irregularity to keep it alive.

It was the last of Morison's significant revivals, although he eontinued to advise the Corporation for many ycars, keeping his office at Monotype even after his plaee was taken by John Dreyfus in 1954. The list given here is by no means complete: there were several other revivals done at the same time, notably Walbaum (1933), Van Dijek (1937), and Bulmer, first cut for the Limited Editions Club in 1937, though not issued to the trade until 1967. The comprchensiveness of thc range does, I hope, justify coneentrating on the story of the Monotype programme at the expense of its eompetitors, for it illustrates the way a major manufaeturer faced the ehallenge of equipping its maehincs with a wide variety of elassie designs. As we have seen, other manufacturers did the same, sometimes before Monotype; and, as later ehapters will show, new designs were cut as well as old.

Although the general poliey of historical recuttings was pursued largely in response to the demands of the printing trade, Morison's role being primarily in reeommending the best types to be followed, grumbles were occasionally heard, especially from Jan van Krimpen, that not enough new designs were cut.

In 1955 Van Krimpen wrote a memorandum in whieh he put in an extremely purist form the case against the Monotype programme of revivals, seizing for his text on the account of them that Morison had written in A tally of types. Van Krimpen wrote (and he wrote in English):

'I need hardly say here once more that I am no friend of copying or even adapting historical type faces . . . for no other reason than that they are neither flesh nor fish: they are not, nor can they be, the thing they pretend to be; they only have a mock flavour of antiquity because of the many violations of the nature of the machine  the punch-cutting machine, that is, in this instance — and the many concessions made to the limitations of the Monotype composing machine; all of these violations and concessions being unavoidable in the process of making them fit for modern use. And, on the other hand, they are not actually products of our time because of the mock flavour of antiquity that has to be aimed at and retained or they could not be spoken of as copies or adaptations; nor could they be named, as it is done, after their models.'

He went on to quote Morison's justification of the early Monotype reuttings as taking a step baekards in order to go forward, and commented acidly, 'Particularly in Great Britain, so many steps backward had already been made, by William Morris and his disciples, that I take the liberty to doubt whether any more of such steps were really nccessary.'

Van Krimpen singled out for particular obloquy the cutting of Poliphilus, which was a careful reproduction of Aldus's original. Of such reproductions he complained,

'Not only that they pretend to be, or are hoped to look, old while in fact they are new; but over and above this ambiguity they are given a look of being handiwork, inasmuch as they follow painstakingly the engraver's and even the typefounder's irregularities . . . while in fact they are made by machine. And this I do not hesitate to call  but may it be well understood: philosophically speaking  dishonesty.'

The other type Van Krimpen specifically objected to was Monotype Ccntaur, of which a foundry version had already existed. The recutting had been supervised by Rogers in person, and Van Krimpen's objection was that he had instructed the Monotype works to keep the irregularitics of the hand-cut type (although the 'hand cutting' was in fact done pantographically by Robert Wiebking of Chicago, to whom Rogers presumably gave the same instructions). Van Krimpen wrote, 'This should, I think, be taken for an objectionably arbitrary piece of conduct [given] the size of the drawings and the great flexibility of the punchcutting machine; a cheap proceeding of arbitrarily determining one's own chosen "imperfcctions" while, when punchcs are being cut by hand, one has to wait and see how the punchcutter's genuine imperfections will look.'

Neither of the two faces he particularly disliked was Morison's concern. Poliphilus was, as we have seen, cut for the Medici Society's facsimile, so that thc 'dishonest' finish was part of the design brief; and Centaur was Rogers's type from first to last. Morison's reply to Van Krimpen was good-humoured and mostly beside the point. However, he dissociated himself from Pierpont's rapture over Poliphilus, and said firmly that there was nothing mock-antique about Bembo.

There was really no answer he could make. The issue which divided the two men is at the root of the argumcnt about revivals. Each of them had a profound sense of the history of letterforms; but Van Krimpen felt that the types of the past should be preserved only in the medium for which they were designcd, and each new step in technology required ncw designs, while Morison on the other hand believed that some types represented ideal forms which could survive the translation to new machines and be as valid as thcy ever were.

Another familiar criticism of revivals came from Updike in a letter of 1924:

'I am certain that after the uproar has ceased over decoration and historical styles, and we have begun to take them more as matters of course, we shall ultimately find that they are only fitted for work of a limited nature, and shall have to invent new types of a more modern feeling, or else use more colourless types which, since they do not reflect any particular period of their own, will be less "in the way" of the mind when we print works by modern authors.'

Time has proved Updike only partly correct. The best Monotype revivals, Baskerville, Bembo, Ehrhardt and Garamond, and to a lesser extent Bell and Fournier, have survived without any historical allusiveness standing 'in the way', just as Imprint and Plantin, less historically correct, have done. This is because in a sense their history has become 'colourless'. At the same time, most modcrn designers, as we shall see, have found it hard to blot out the past completely, and even when preparing fresh faces have, unwittingly or not, kept history in the corner of their eyes. As Goudy said, 'The old fellows stole most of our best ideas.'

## The designers - 1

If somc diffcultis of approch arose in the daptation of historical types for modern printing methods, therc were others whcn ncw designs came to be cut. Almost every way of translating the idca ofa leter into metal type was followcd by one or othcr of thc designers who appear in this chapter. Koch cut his own punchcs for somc of his types, believed by somc to be the ideal way of proceeding; Van Krimpen closcly supervised a skilled punchcuttcr, P. H. Rädisch, in thc production of all his faces in thcir first foundry versions; Goudy engraved the matrices of his later types himself on a pantographic machinc; Dwiggins drew accurate full-scale patterns for the pantograph to follow; Morison cmploycd a draftsman, Victor Lardent, to draw Timcs New Roman; and Gill, by tradc a letter carver rather than a type dcsigner, drew mostly frcehand alphabets for the drawing officcs of Monotype and the Caslon foundry to convert into type.

However, dcsigners of vcry diffcrent kinds secm to be united in disliking thc mechanical look of lettcrs given too much finish by drawing offices left to their own devices, and many havc strugglcd hard to prevent its happcning to their typcs. The ethical qucstion they faced, and still face, is whether, if machines can produce theoretically 'pcrfect' lctters, they ought to be allowed to. It is a problem stated explicitly by Gill, writing about Joanna, and, as we have seen, by Van Krimpen; and it was answcrcd tacitly by many morc designcrs, largely in the negative. Goudy, Rogers, Dwiggins, and many morc all carefully supervised the inclusion of deliberate irregularities in their types to counteract the sanitising tcndency of the mechanical engravcr. Van Krimpen, who disapproved of such artificial imperfcction, observcd a progress towards lifclessness in thc developing stages of his own drawings, and suggested\* that the vigorous pencil outlincs should be followed by the pantograph operator rather than the later, more polishcd ink drawing. By this mcans he believed that vivacity could be retaincd with no sacrifice of honesty: the kind of irregularity which the hand punchcutter introduced naturally would be therc in the drawing, and not added artificially. But this argument really only moves the problem one step back, sincc a deliberate selection of a preferred 'imperfection' is still being made. The qucstion remains one about the difficulties of wider choice introduced by ncw machines, which exist in many fields beside type dcsign.

The designers who follow, in thc order they were born, had their different answers to the difficulties, somc conscious and somc intuitive. It is fitting that the story should begin in the United States, whcrc the new machines were inventcd which transformed the type dcsigner's craft.

# Frederic W. Goudy

Goudy was the oldest, one of the most prolific, and possibly the most dedicated of the great innovative type designers of this century. After a slow and uncertain start, and apart from his writings on the alphabet and lettering, and a small amount of printing conceived chiefly as a vehicle for his own creations, he gave himself single-mindedly to the drawing and manufacture of typc. He occupied a pivotal position between the passing world of the craftsmen who cut punches by hand, and the new breed of type draftsmen. In this he worked to some extent against the grain of the century, for he began as a freelance designer sending his drawings to foundries for a fee, and only later became increasingly involved in the manufacture of his types. He then supervised the engraving of matrices by a craftsman, Robert Wiebking of Chicago, commissioned thc casting, and marketed the founts himself; and shortly before Wiebking's death in 1927, he took up his own matrix cngraving and casting. On a celcbrated day, Thanksgiving, 1933, he showed the advantages of his method. Bruce Rogers had come to lunch, and was complaining that he could find no capitals exactly the right size and style for a title page he had in hand. His host suggested one of his own faces, Goudy Antique, went out to his workshop, engraved the necessary matrices in the intermediate size wanted, cast the type, and handed the sorts to Rogers as he left the house. 'Some service', hc commented drily.

He was extraordinarily productive. In his own list of designs, set down at the end of his lifc in the Typophile Chap Book\* devoted to his work, he included wcll over a hundred entries, and though he counted roman and italic as two designs, it remains a formidablc achievement. Many of the types are similar in conception, sevcral are not very good, and some are so terrible that cven their author, inclined to view his own work with a favourable eye, did not reproduce them in A half-century; but the ones that remain are enough to place Goudy among the handful of designers who have changed the look of the types wc read. His faces are completely individual and, with the exception of his one historical recreation, Garamont, could not be by anyone else.

Goudy did not shrink from sclf-promotion, and attracted critics, one of whom sourly commented, 'It's all Goudy thin and Goudy fat, Goudy this and Goudy that.' But we should remember that he was marketing his types, and needed to sell them to eat. The name became a selling point, and so naturally he used it.

Goudy was born on 8 March 1865 in Bloomington, Illinois, the first of three designers to be born in the mid-west within fifteen years of each other, all of whom went east and betwccn them changed the face of American book design: the other two were Bruce Rogers and Dwiggins. His father was an inspector of schools, and his childhood was spent in various small towns around the state. While quite young he showed an aptitude for lettcring, and was also much impressed by the sight of a pantograph, which an itinerant artist was demonstrating as a drawing aid. At the age of twenty-four he went to Chicago, and began a series of clerking jobs, one of which was for a bookshop; it lcd him to the rare book department of A. C. McClurg, where over the years he could look at the new publications of the English private presses, Kelmscott, Doves, Eragny and Vale, as thcy appeared.

He proved better at losing his clerking jobs than keeping them, and at the end of one he and a friend began a small printing business, the Booklet, later renamed the Camelot Press. 'This, mind you,' Goudy wrote later, 'at a time when I hardly knew which end of a type was the printing end.' In spite of his protested innocencc, thc design of the Press's work was noticed by the publishers Stone and Kimball, who asked it to set some issues of their magazine, The chap book. Soon, however, Goudy sold his share and in 1896, while waiting for his next job, drew a set of capital letters one evening in his room. Not quite knowing what to do with them, he sent them to the Dickinson Type Foundry in Boston, part of thc recently formed Amcrican Type Foundcrs group, from whom hc had bought type. Thc foundry accepted the dcsign, callcd Camclot, and the ncxt onc he sent them. Neither showed conspicuous promise, and both were distinctly of the 189os. Nevcrtheless, the very fact they were cut is significant, for as Goudy wrote, 'We must remember that original type designs were not at that time regular items of purchase by type foundries; most of thcir designs werc produced by their own punchcuttcrs, and wcrc usually mere variations only of the types of other foundries.'

![](images/7ca227ed780a721a32c949145e4885bfb332468f10f73bd3dadb7aafed46a49c.jpg)  
Frederic W. Goudy (18651947). By courtesy of the Cary Collection, Rochester Institute of Technology, New York.

After a short spell in Detroit working as a cashier for a weekly magazine, The Michigan farmer, Goudy finally decided that such work was not for him, and set up as a freelancc lettering artist in Chicago, working for a number of department stores as well as for Herbert S. Stone, whosc partnership with Kimball had ended amicably; Kimball had gonc to New York to set up the Chcltenham Press, which commissioned the typc of the same name. Goudy also joined the teaching staff of the new Frank Holme school of illustration as a lettering tutor.

During this time, Goudy designcd a few proprietary faces for local businesses. In 1903 one of them, commissioned by a department store but found by them to be too expensive to cut and cast, became the house type of his second printing venture, the Village Press, which he set up with the collaboration of Will

In spitc of its dcbt to the English private press types, in particular to Charles Ricketts's Vale type ('What an ancestry for an advertising face!' commented Goudy while it was still intended for thc Kuppenheimer store), and the consequcnt quaintness of appcarancc in our eycs, Goudy considered Village as the first of his types to show maturity.

Within a ycar, Goudy read about a crafts society at Hingham, a small town outside Boston, Massachusetts; he was himself bccoming increasingly fircd by craft ideals, and although he had built up a rcgular clientele in Chicago, hc moved to Hingham in 1904. He was joined shortly by Dwiggins, whom hc had taught at thc Frank Holme school, but whereas Dwiggins stayed on for thc rest of his life, Goudy found it difficult to break down Bostonian social barriers and to cstablish himself profcssionally, and movcd to New York in 1906. Here, on 10 January 1908, his workshop and the Village Press's equipment were destroyed in the first of two disastrous fires he suffrcd in his lifetimc. Paradoxically, howevcr, the destruction of the Vilage Press, which had been a drain on his pocket, left him financially bctter off.

Goudy now turned almost exclusively to the design of typc. He was commissioned to produce a roman for the American Lanston Monotype Company, his initiation into working with the limitations of the Monotype diecase; the typc was briefly called Goudy Old Style against his wishes, a name which was given to another face before attaching itself to the type we know by it today. This first face was later given the unromantic title of 38-E. Next, Goudy was given the opportunity of designing his first major typeface. In 1911 the New York publisher Mitchcll Kennerley invited him to design an edition of H. G. Wells's story The door in the mall. They had spccimen pages set in 18pt Caslon, but Goudy was dissatisfied with them, and suggested to Kennerley that he should design a new type for the book, which they could market later to help with the costs. Kenncrley agreed, and Goudy, inspircd at first by the Oxford University Press Fell types, but later shedding the influence as he bcgan to draw, produced Kennerley Old Style, engraved and cast by Wicbking, and sold in founts by Goudy at his Village Type Foundry. The year after Kennerley, another commissioned type was abandoned, this time beeause of the death of the sponsor, and so Goudy marketed it himself. On the appearanee of the later type, it was renamed Goudy Antique (another name whieh was used again later), and then, when eut by Ameriean Monotype, Goudy Lanston; and as if eonfusion had not already made its masterpieee, Caslon in England issued a version and ealled it Ratdolt.

Goudy was unhappy with Caslon's cutting and their name, but nevertheless he went to England in 1914, intending to visit them and interest them in eutting his next type. Although Caslon eould not undertake the work, beeause of the approaehing war and the likely requisitioning of their faetory for munitions work, they were interested in buying Goudy's designs, and their enthusiasm so impressed Clarenee Marder of ATF, who was with Goudy, that on their return to the United States he persuaded Robert Nelson, the president of ATF, to commission a new Goudy type. Thus at last the face now known as Goudy Old Style was designed and eut.

Goudy was at pains to restrain the desire of the ATF drawing office to modify his design, and Nelson backed him, although in the end he was persuaded to allow shorter deseenders than his judgement suggested, to the detriment of the type. With a eharaeteristie mixture of braggadocio and modesty he wrote, 'The faee, as finally produeed, was almost as great an innovation as my Kennerley . . . I am almost satisfied that the design is a good one.' It was certainly the most suecessful of his types, though one of its best variants, Goudy Catalogue, was aetually drawn by Morris Benton. It still shows the rather fluid outlines ofhis early letters, but there is enough tautness to give it strength.

types. Two faces drawn for ATF to fulfil a contractual arrangement, Booklet Old Style and the unattractively named Goudytype, were competent, and the latter rather better than its author secmed to think it; but National Old Style for the National Biscuit Company has little to recommend it.

Two display types followed, both in 1918: the handsome, classically inspired Hadriano, and the refined Goudy Open. The second was an inline type based on lettering engraved on a French print, and had the unusual result of prompting an unadorned version, Goudy Modern. Decorated faces normally follow the plain parent design, not the other way about. Goudy Modern is a crisp and distinguished lctter, and one of his best; when cut by English Monotype in 1928, it was wcll uscd by Francis Meynell at the Nonesuch Press.

In 1920 Goudy was askcd by J. Maury Dove, president of the Lanston Monotype Company of Philadelphia, to advise thcm on design, thus anticipating by about three years the similar decision of thc English company to appoint Stanley Morison. Thc first fruit of the partnership was the Garamont already described; the second was Italian Old Style. Monotype had a customcr who was prcpared to buy their machinery, but wanted to be able to set in Morris Benton's Cloister, designed for ATF. Dove wanted to adapt ATF's design for the Monotype. Goudy argued that this was unethical, and Dove that it was common practice; but he eventually yielded to Goudy's suggestion of a new face based freely on the same Venetian models. The result was Italian Old Style (a different face from English Monotype's own earlier type of the same name, Series 108), which is one of Goudy's best book faces.

On his own behalf he designed Goudy Newstyle (1921), suggested originally by the writings on phonetics of the English Poet Laureate Robert Bridges. The type was equipped with twenty alternative sorts intended to clarify the noriously illogical spelling of the English language; but, like most such experiments, it did not catch on, and the unaugmented type, handsome though it is, was used only by a few private presses, most magnificently by the Grabhorn Press of San Francisco in their edition of Whitman's Leaves of grass (1930).

Newstyle was the last major face engravcd for Goudy by Robert Wicbking, who died in 1927. In 1923 the Goudy family moved from New York to a watermill up the Hudson river at Marlboro, and with

Type design moves at the pace of the most conservative reader', Morison wrote. 'The good type-designer therefore realises that, for a new fount to be successful, it has to be so good that only a few recognise its novelty. If readers do not notice the consummate reticence and rare discipline of a new type, it is probably a good letter. But if my friends think that the tail of my lower-case r or the lip of my lower-case e is rather jolly, you may know that the fount would have been better had neither been made. A type which is to have anything like a present, let alone a future, will neither be very "different" nor very "jolly".

Goudy drily observed in Typolologia, 'I am not sure that I accept his dictum completely'. In fact he did not aeeept it at all: Goudy's types are individual, always recognisable, with charaeteristies which can sometimes become irritating. The first type for whieh he cut matrices was intended for the private press run by Spencer Kellogg. He began in 1925, and his efforts to master the new technique took so long that Kellogg tired of printing and cancelled the order. This design, originally ealled Aries, was later reworked and recut and called Franciscan, the private type of the Grabhorn Press (1932). It was the first of a linc of blackletter and semi-blackletter faccs which Goudy worked on intermittcntly for the rcst of his life, and which he often confessed to preferring to his romans. Goudy was aggrieved to find it described as his last typc, but it is hardly surprising.

While he was enjoying himself with blackletter, Goudy was also designing more romans. In 1927 he cut Deepdene, having first offered it to Monotype, who were not then intercsted in a new book face. Deseribing his type, Goudy wrote,

'It is hardly possible to create a good type face that will differ radically from the established forms of the past: nevertheless it is still possible to secure new expressions of life and vigor. The types in daily use, almost without exception, betray too fully the evidences of their origin, and do not always follow the best traditions. It requires the skilled hand, the appreciation and taste of the artist, and the trained mind of the student to select suitable models which may be adapted to our use and to which we may give new graces suited to our times. I have made designs that reverted for their inspiration to the lapidary characters of the early Romans; others that were based on the classic types of Jenson, Ratdolt, Aldus; still others that were suggested by the scribes' hands which were also the source of the types of those masters; and now, in the autumn of my labors, I draw with practically no reference to any of the sources mentioned; relying largely on the broad impressions of early forms stored up by years of study and practice, and governed by a technical knowledge of the requirements of type founding and typography, 1 attempt to create those impressions into new designs of beauty and utility.'

Goudy died in 1947, heaped with honours, and has kept his plaee as one of the first and most original of the new kind of type designer who emerged with the new century. His faees are not widely used for the setting of books: they do not fulfil the eustomary demands of retieenee for sueh purposes. But for displayed work and advertising design they have always been deservedly popular. The old fellows may have stolen all his best ideas, as he used to maintain, but he certainly stole them back again.

Born five years after Goudy, Rogers nevertheless went east beforc him, and unlike him had a sueess as a young man which made his later life seem to him an anticlimax. Whereas Goudy was a type designer who occasionally produced books, Rogers was the other way about: a book designer whose attention to the minutiae of his work led him oecasionally to the design of type. Updike ealled him 'the most distinguished designer of books of our time', and Meynell went further: 'the greatcst artifieer of the book who ever lived'. Frederie Warde,\* the designer of the Arrighi italie which aecompanies Rogers's Centaur in its Monotype eutting, dubbed him 'prematurely an old master'. Working always in close contaet with a number of well-known printing houses in the United States and England, and benefiting from what Warde called their 'leisurely idealism', Rogers designed his books meticulously and supervised every detail of their produetion with painstaking care, even in some cases down to the smell of the ink. He was a perfectionist who out of a total of some seven hundred books ehose only thirty that he was pleased with. But that list ineluded several which are classics of design, and chief among them was the Oxford Lectern Bible (1935), one of the great books of the century.

His quest for perfection as a designer led him to mix types from different founts to get the effects he wanted, and to design special characters. In one ease he produced a modified Caslon by rubbing down both the face and the sides of an existing fount, to darken the face and fit the letters eloser together; new matrices were then made electrolytieally and founts of 'Riverside Caslon' (19o9) east from them.

It was inevitable that sueh attention to detail would lead Rogers to design type, and his major aehievement in this field, Centaur, has been deseribed by Professor Herbert Johnson as 'perhaps the finest of the five or six great typefaees designed by Amerieans.

Bruee Rogers was born in Lafayette, Indiana, on 14 May 187o. His family eame of English Yorkshire stock, and he maintained a lasting affeetion for England, where he did mueh of his best work, though it was not unmixed with dislike of the diseomfort of aetually living there. At the age of sixteen he went to Purdue University in Lafayette, studied art, and after graduation became a newspaper artist, in thc days before journalistic photography, and then for a short period a landscape painter. Already at university he had shown a flair for lettering; now his feeling for the dcsign of books was stimulated, as so many others had been and were to be, by the sight of the Kelmscott Press books whieh were just beginning to appear. They were bought direet from Morris by Rogers's friend J. M. Bowles, who worked in an Indianapolis art shop and produced a magazine, Modern art, as a sideline. Rogers, who had begun drawing lettering for publishers, also contributed work to the magazine, and when in 1895 Bowles and Modern art were taken to Boston by the Prang chromolithography firm, and needed a designer, Rogers joined him. The work was leisurely, which suited Rogers. 'He was guaranteed at least \$1o a week, whieh he made oceasionally', Bowles remembered. 'He ean live on peanuts and popeorn for as long as necessary; not only ean he live the simple life but he aetually enjoys it.'

A year after his arrival in Boston, Rogers was invited by George H. Mifflin to join Houghton Mifflin at their Rivcrside Press as designer, and in 1900 he persuaded Mifflin to set up within the Press a department for the produetion of fine books for the colleetor. A number of Rogers's finest books appearcd as Riverside Press editions, and he was doubtless influenccd by the example of Updike at the Merrymount Press close by, who had been produeing handsome books sinee 1893, some of them in an historieally eeleetic style whieh, Warde remarked, had been found in the nursery and left in the drawing room. Rogers paid elose attention to each titlc, foreshadowing in the variety and subtle allusiveness of his books the work of Meynell at the Nonesueh Press twenty years later. Within a fcw years, for the thrce volume folio edition of The essays of Montaigne (1902-4), Rogers designed his first type, ealled Montaigne. It was a refinement of the mueh-copied Jenson letter, whieh Rogers was not entirely pleased with. 'In a way', wrotc Warde, 'the puneheutter had done his work too well; Mr Rogers decided that there had been too mueh "improvement" over the original, and he resolved to return to Jenson at some later time for a more direet inspiration.' He did, a deeade later, and the result was Centaur.

In 1912, after sixteen years at the Rivcrside Press, Rogers decided to leave and become his own master again; and although he sometimes regretted the insecurity, he remaincd a freelance for the rest of his life. He spent the summer of 1912 in England, the

An earlier visit Rogers made to England, shortly after the first eutting of foundry Centaur, was in 1916. It was the middle of World War I, and the journey was made at the invitation of Emery Walker, with the aim of printing fine editions in the tradition of Kelmseott and Doves. He took with him a commission from the Grolier Club of New York for an edition of Durer's On the just shaping of letters, whieh was the first and only book printed by the Mall Press. Most of Walker's press workers had been ealled up, so that the aetual printing was largely done by Rogers himself, learning as he went, in a eold Hammersmith basement in the early months of 1917.

The assoeiation with Walker ended, Rogers was invited at the suggestion of Sydney Coekerell, formerly Morris's seeretary at the Kelmseott Press and now direetor of the Fitzwilliam Museum at Cambridge, to eome to advise the Cambridge University Press on its typography. Rogers's report has beeome a well-known analysis, and was extremely unflattering. The reforms he proposed had to wait until after the aftermath of the war and its attendant shortages, but led to the appointment of Stanley Morison as typographieal adviser to the Press under the next University Printer, Walter Lewis. While in Cambridge, Rogers designed a poster type for Meynell's Peliean Press, whieh was eut in wood, somewhat unskilfully beeause of the shortage of eraftsmen. Warde wrote, it is hoped by those who have seen the original drawings that one day all the letters will be reeut competently'.

Rogers returned to the United States in 1919, and spent the next deeade on book design, notably for William E. Rudge at Mount Vernon, outside New York. But the three books whieh are by general eonsent his greatest were still to eome, and dated from the same stay in England which began with the supervision of Monotype Centaur, in whieh all three were set. T. E. Lawrenee (of Arabia) had made a translation of Homer's Odyssey at Rogers's suggestion, whieh was printed at Emery Walker's works under Rogers's eyes; then there was another eommission from the Grolier Club to design Stanley Morison's essay on Fra Luca de Pacioli, the Italian writing master; and there was his masterpiece, the Oxford Leetern Bible. It was set in 22pt Centaur with slightly shortened extenders, after trials with Bembo and Goudy Newstyle. (The latter type was used later, in a version modified by Rogers in eollaboration with Sol Hess of Lanston Monotype and called Goudy Bible, for the World Bible of 1949.)

The Leetern Bible has been eritiised for not beig monumental enough, but this was Rogers's deelared intention: he wanted it to look as if he 'was aeeustomed to knoeking off large folios daily, or at least weckly, as mere routine work'.\* While it is too grand really to give that impression, it has an agreeable lack of pomposity together with that grandeur, and though he designed many more books, he was never to reach such heights again.

Bruce Rogers died at his house in New Fairfield, Cnett seventh birthday.

![](images/f2d6582f3cddc1e2177898b25a9d7093972e75f277ce3200c708bd25d370ddad.jpg)  
Rudolf Koch (18761934), photographed in 1931.

The new generation of type designers in Germany, like their counterparts in the United States, saw their faces appear first as foundry type for hand setting; but here it took longer for the designs to be adapted for mechanical composition, sometimes until the arrival of filmsetting: many of them were blackletter founts, since blaekletter was the standard seript used in German printing up to the early 194os, and so the costly adaptation for Monotype or Linotype maehines could not be supported by large overseas sales. Some of the new faces were experimental hybrids between blackletter and roman, and a few were romans, though most of these were idiosyneratie renderings of the elassie letter as seen through the eyes of designers who were not altogether at home with it.

Another differenee betwveen Germany and the English-spcaking countries was that the German traditional foundries were far more energetie in solieiting new designs from artists who were not specialist typographers. Typieal was the well-established Bauer foundry of Frankfurt, which confronted the ehallenge of meehanieal eomposition direetly in the area where the new manufaeturers were most laggard. The foundry commissioned many faees from teachers and artists, notably Lueian Bernhard's family of types bearing his name (1912 on), Paul Renner's Futura (1928 on), Imre Reiner's Corvinus (1934) and the odd, but still popular Weiss Antiqua designed in 1926 by the distinguished graphie artist Emil Rudolf Weiss (1875-1942), who also contributed a Fraktur blaekletter and some attraetive type ornaments. On the whole Bauer deliberately eoneentrated on roman faces, issuing alongside these types new cuttings of older classies such as Bodoni (1926) and the Egyptian Beton (1936), and they competed hard in the foreign market, opening a New York office in 1927.

The professors and industrial designers from whom the big firms commissioned their flagship faces relied on the foundry eraftsmen to turn their drawings into type. A conspieuous exeeption to this pattern was the eareer of Rudolf Koeh, who spent most of his working life as a staff designer at the small Klingspor foundry in Offenbach.

This business,\* sinee 1841 ealled the Rudhard foundry, had been bought in 1892 by a wealthy manufaeturer for his two sons, Karl, then aged twenty-four, and Wilhelm Klingspor; they ehanged its name in 1906. Wilhelm Klingspor died in 1924, and it was Karl who was the driving foree in the imaginative type design programme the foundry undertook, who set up a finc printing office to advertise their types, and established the 'Klingspor style'. He died in 1950.

The first fruits of Klingspor's new policy appeared in 19oo: designs by Otto Eekmann, who died two years later aged thirty-seven, and Peter Behrens, the distinguished architeet and industrial designer. Both faces were Jugendstil curiosities, and caused a considerable stir at the time; they represented a eonseious effort to escape from the domination of blackletter. They can still sometimes be found in the catalogues of dry-transfer lettering, and Eekmann is available on Linotype digital machines. The puneheutter of the original type was Louis Hoell, who afterwards moved to the Bauer foundry, where he eut the Bremer Press type, the designs of E. R. Weiss, and Joseph Blumenthal's Spiral/Emerson. Behrens went on to produee a roman and italie (1907-8) which show elearly the diffeulties German designers found in taekling Renaissanee seripts. Klingspor also used the heraldie artist Otto Hupp, who produeed a handsome condensed blackletter Liturgiseh (1906),

During Koch's ycars at the foundry, Klingspor's chief outside designer was Walter Tiemann (1876- 195 1), a co-founder of Germany's first private press, the Janus Press (19o7), with thc distinguished printer Carl Ernst Poeschel. Ticmann was a paintcr and a tcacher: he was the director of the Leipzig academy of graphic art and book crafts when Jan Tschichold studied there. His typcs were nowhere near as original as Koch's, and suffer from comparison with them. Ticmann's Mediäval (1909) is scarcely more convincing than Behrens's, though the italic (1912) shows interesting parallels with the Cranach Press italic which Edward Johnston was designing at the same time. Narziss (192 1) had a considcrable succcss in advertising display work, and Tiemann Antiqua (1923), a Didone which shows an inclination towards French refincment without the neccssary knowledge of the letter forms, has recently becn adapted by Adrian Frutigcr for Linotype. Tiemann's later faces, Orpheus (1928), Daphnis (1931) and Offizin (1952), did not maintain even this standard.

In the first decades of the century, there was a strong English influence in German fine printing, encouragcd by men like Harry Kessler, both at his Cranach Press, which in many ways was an English private press which happened to be in Weimar, and earlier in his use of Edward Johnston and Eric Gill to draw lettering for books published by the Insel-Verlag of Leipzig. Willy Wiegand's Bremer Press, which ran from 191 1 t0 1939, used the designer Anna Simons, who had been a pupil of Johnston's, and her 1910 German translation of Johnston's Writing and illuminating and lettering (first published in 1906), was widely influential. But already in 1903, Rudolf Koch, then a struggling young designer in Leipzig, had begun experimenting with a broad pen, which set him on the way to mastery of many calligraphic styles.

Koch was born on 20 November 1876 in Nuremberg, which had been one of the first great centres of printing, and the home of Albrecht Dürer. His father, a sculptor and inspector of museums, had died when Koch was only ten, and the reduced family circumstances which resulted meant that the boy had to leave school early and take up an apprenticcship as a metal worker in Hanau, near Offenbach and Frankfurt, while studying at the art school in his spare time. ambition to be an artist grew, and before his four year apprenticeship was completed, hc returned to Nuremberg to study to be an art teacher at the arts and crafts school, of which he later wrote that it was 'rather a refuge for aged but deserving professors than an institution for the education of young people'. He moved to Munich, but shortly before he was to take his examinations he found that his apprenticcship, being outside Bavaria, could not count towards his qualification; so, in 1898, he went to Leipzig to work as a designer with various printers and binders, and from 19o2 on as a freelance. His style at that time was the Jugendstil then in fashion, which he quickly left behind. 'May God forgive me for the things I produeed in those years', he commented afterwards.

In the autumn of 1905, Koch saw an advertisement in the magazine Kuust uud Dekoration for a post at the Rudhard foundry, shortly to be renamed Klingspor. He joined them in 1906, and stayed there until his death in 1934. Hc began in a relatively humble capacity, but Karl Klingspor soon recognised his talents as a penman, and he began to design types. At the same time he was appointed as a teacher of lettering at the Offenbach technical institute, where later, in 1921, he set up an important workshop for students.

All Koch's types werc designed for Klingspor, with the exception of the Deutsche Anzcigenschrift family done between 1923 and 1934 directly for Stempel, who had a controlling interest in Klingspor from 1918 onwards. In 1929, when he was bcing wooed by an American foundry, Koch paid this tribute to Karl Klingspor: 'If I have produced a useful type design, it is in large measure due to the proprietor of the Klingspor foundry, whose advisory rôle in cach task I have undertaken for the firm has amounted to a collaboration. My relationship with Klingspor goes far beyond a question of business, and I will never seek another.'

More than half of Koch's types were blackletter faces, to which he brought a rough calligraphic strength rarely seen before in type. Almost all of them grew from his own styles of writing, practised for long periods of preparation beforc thc type itsclf was designcd, frequently in quotations from the Bible, since Koch was a deeply religious man who, like Eric Gill, tricd to unite life and work in an harmonious whole. His first type, called simply Deutsche Schrift, was a bold blackletter which occupied him until 1910, and its narrow version until 1913. Koch's army serviee as a grenadier was a painful timc for him. He saw action on the Frcnch and Russian fronts as a privatc soldier, spent seven months in an army hospital in Offcnbach during 1916 before being sent baek to the front, and finally was diseharged because of ill health in October 1917. Thc war years saw the dcsign, and the next fcw ycars the cutting, of a new and original developmcnt in his work, the delieate roman and italic called Koeh Antiqua and Kursiv. One of thc most sueeessful advcrtising faees of the intcr-war period, still often uscd to suggest the vanishing luxury of ocean lincrs, it neverthelcss grew dircetly from Koeh's calligraphy, whieh he uscd in texts eelebrating the joys of domesticity. As well as the roman and italie, there was also a Grobe, or heavy weight.

After thc war, Koch's most productivc period began. But this blackletter tradition was being ehallenged within Germany from a powerful ncw quarter: not the humanist roman designs of the Italian Renaissance, but the modernists of the Bauhaus, who advocated the universal aeeeptance of sans-serif types. The kind of gcometrical design proposed, which found its eomplete expression in Futura, was on thc face of it antithetical to Koch's intuitivc approach, and he refuscd on principle to draw one. Koch felt the need to stretch bcyond the blackletter tradition in other directions, and also to try his hand at cutting his own punches. As a first attempt which would not stretch his punchcutting skills too far, hc produced Neuland (1923), a brutal sans-serif again based on his rough pen-drawn lettcring, again a popular advcrtising type (imitated by Monotype and callcd Othello), and again used with imagination by Francis Meynell, in thc Noncsuch Prcss Genesis (1924), wherc it goes well with Paul Nash's powerful woodcuts.

In the Pctcr Jessen Schrift, called aftcr thc director of the Berlin muscum of arts and crafts whose lecturcs had influcnccd many Gcrman graphic designers, Koch returncd to a simplificd blackletter, with romanised capitals for usc with the gothic lower-case for the first time. The initial impetus for the type was a great edition of the four Gospcls printcd at the Klingspor Press in 1926. It remaincd a popular face in the Koch circle, and was bought by the Grabhorn Press of San Francisco, who called it Koch Bibcl Gotisch, and first used it for thcir handsome edition of Mandeville's Travels for Random House in 1928. Koch himself cut the punches of two sizes, except for the 12pt capitals, which were cut by his son Paul; thc other sizes werc cut by Gustav Eichenauer.

Koch's experiments with capitals continucd with Wallau (193o), called after a Mainz printcr who in 1885 had anticipated William Morris in suggesting the rcvival of the round blackletter script known as Rotunda, common among the printing types of the first centuries after the invention of printing, and including those of Anton Koberger, thc great printer of Koch's native Nuremberg. This kind of design had prompted Morris's Troy and Chaucer types, and latcr influenced Eric Gill's Jubilee (1935), but none of those can match the calligraphic verve of Koch's drawing. For Wallau he made two sets of capitals, blacklctter and romanised, and the latter were used more often. They and the lighter Offenbach roman capitals (193o), designed to accompany a blackletter lowcr case, were of a pcn-formed sans-scrif design which strongly influenced the capitals of pupils and admirers. Although their lower-case characters were quite distinct, ATF Lydian (1938-46) by the American Warren Chappcll, who had studied with Koch in the early 193os, and Monotype Klang (1955) by Will Cartcr, who had spent some months at Paul Koch's workshop in 1938, both show Koch's clear influcnce. Koch designed two seript types, an uninspired brush-formed latin seript called Holla (1932), and a complicated face derived from German handwriting, Koch Kurrent, whieh because of the diffieulties of its cutting was not finished until 1935, after his death.

The last type eut by Koch, in one size only, was a curious gawky roman called Marathon (begun 1930; other sizes finished 1938) which cannot be ealled a sueeess; but from his designs his son Paul cut the beautiful dancing blackletter Claudius (1931-7), drawn with great dash, which is a more fitting end to the career of this inspired and inspiring designer. Koch died on 9 April 1934, but his work was carried on by Paul Koch, who ran a workshop in the old quarter of Frankfurt, called the Haus zum Fürsteneck, where the young Hermann Zapf, among others, came to study. Paul Koch disappeared, and was presumed killed, on the Russian front in 1943.

Rudolf Koch was driven by a religious sense of purpose, and, combining as he did the talents of ealligrapher and puneheutter, ought theoretieally to have produced perfeet types. That he did not do so is due partly to his calligraphy being too free, and his punchcutting too rough, and partly to his belonging to a graphic tradition outside the European mainstream. Nonetheless, we can recognise his anachronistie, personal and undeniably magnificent achievement, whieh should be kept in mind by type designers whenever they find thcir work becoming anaemie: for that is something Koeh's types never were.

Ten years younger than Rogers, and fifteen younger than Goudy, Dwiggins also camc late to the dcsign of books and type. He was an original and exuberant graphic artist with an instantly reeognisable style, who ncvertheless, when he came to type design, produced a handful of faees which are remarkable for thcir comparative sobriety.\*

Dwiggins was born in Martinsvillc, Ohio, in 1880, the son of a doetor. His interest in art took him at the age of ninetecn to Chieago, where he went to Frank Holme's sehool and studied lettering with Goudy. After a brief,nsusful spell as a jobbing prit rural Ohio, he followcd Goudy to Hingham, Massaehusetts; and when Goudy moved on to New York, he stayed on at Hingham for the rest of his life, though for many years he also had a studio in Boston. A prolific worker, he was, aecording to his wife, 'a man who loved his work so mueh, that no day was long enough'; indeed he often maintained that his aim was to campaign for a forty-eight hour day. In addition to his lettering and illustration, he ran an ambitious marionette theatre, for whieh he made stage, sets and east, and wrote the plays.

Dwiggins's early graphie work was for advertising, but he came inereasingly to prefer designing books. In 1923 he met the publisher Alfred Knopf, for whom he was to design close on three hundred books over the years, and established the Borzoi visual stylc indelibly. But it was for another publisher, Random House, that he designed his most celebrated edition, H. G. Wells's The time machine (1931), full of his characteristic vignettes, whieh wcre built up by thc skillcd application of stcncilled elcments. Many of his books and jaekets wcrc decorated with his vcry individual ealligraphy, whieh was an eclectic amalgam of late eighteenth and early nineteenth century scripts and romans frcely redrawn with a flcxiblc nib and a lot of dash. In view of his interest in lettering and also in rcpetitive steneilling teehniques, it is surprising that he took so long to take up type design, but he was in his late forties before a chancc observation in his manual Layout in advertising (1928) led to an invitation from Harry L. Gage of the Mergenthaler Linotypc Company. In the book, Dwiggins eriticised eontemporary sans-scrifs, espeeially the capitals, and wrote, 'the type founders will do a serviee if they will provide

Dwiggins himself seemed to have thought that he had succeeded too well,and that the type showed less of the metal shavings part' than originally intended. Nonetheless, it still looks extremely crisp and fresh and, in its author's words, 'moves along the line nieely'.

In 1939, Dwiggins produced Caledonia, his best and most successful type. The design process, as described by Dwiggins in his introduetion to the first speeimen, began with a liking for the Scoteh romans cut by the Wilson foundry of Glasgow in the late eighteenth century, and developed as a cocktail of styles with the addition of elements of the slightly later Bulmer types cut by William Martin. In the end, Dwiggins transeended his models. 'The face as it emerges', he wrote, 'is not at all like Bulmer's Martin nor like Wilson's Scotch, but it has touches of both of them in spots. Also it has something of that simple, hard-working, feet-on-the-ground quality that has kept Seotch Modern in serviee for so many years. About the "liveliness of action" that one sees in the Martin letters, and to a less degree (one modestly says) in Caledonia: that quality is in the curves  the way they get away from the straight stems with a calligraphic flick, and in the nervous angle on the under side of the arches as they descend to the right. The finishing strokes at the bottoms of the letters, cut straight across without "brackets", making sharp angles with the upright stems, add "snap" to many of the old "modern face" designs - and why not to Caledonia?'

Dwiggins took great care with his working drawings, and drew Caledonia at full ten-inch working size. Rudolph Ruzicka, the recipient of Dwiggins's essay on type design, WAD to RR,\* wrote 'I have not the least doubt that Dwiggins eould have aehieved whatever results he wanted by means of any one of the many teehniques he eommanded, yet in Caledonia, roman and italic, there is apparent no pen or any other

a theory that the proponent thinks may have sense in it: Fine type letters were, in the first place, copies of fine written letters. Fine written letters were fine because they were produced in the most direct and simple way by a tool in the hands of a person expert in its use, by a person, moreover, who was an artist, i.e., a person equipped to make sound judgments about lines, curves, proportions, etc. The artist of

ancestral "mark of the tool", and what there is of his individual hand is felt rather than seen. Caledonia has the ease of good clear speech, with just a hint of a pleasant Scotch accent'.

Dwiggins designed several types for Linotype which were not taken beyond the experimental stage, though in a few cases limited editions were printed from them. Stuyvesant, Winchester, Arcadia, Tippecanoe and Hingham ranged from the interesting to the weird, and only Falcon showed real possibilities, rather more than Eldorado (1953) which actually was released. It was Dwiggins's last type. He died on Christmas Day, 1956.

Dwiggins's easy writing style, and the joie de vivre of his graphic work, partly serve to hide his professionalism; but it is precisely that combination of freedom and discipline that makes his types as good as they are. Although he did not cut his types, he made careful pattern drawings and left the cutting to other professionals; he managed to keep the controlled informality of his drawings intact in the final type, and he made it look easy.

# SID HDrRR A leler but desiming Type

![](images/de6e222b1c0a91621bebf4c49eab349e764f1ea5c4a5571b42e868daa49a486f.jpg)

Eric Gill deseribed himself on his own gravestone as a stone carver, which embraced the two activitics for which he was most famous, sculpture and the cutting of inscriptional lettering. He might have added: wood engraver, for he was a versatile and original artist in that medium, and his engravings for the Cranach Press Canticum Canticorum and the Golden Cockerel Press Four Gospels (both 1931) arc magnificent achievements of book illustration. He might also have added: essayist, since he was an indefatigable writer on a great variety of subjeets from sex, polities, religion and the nature of workmanship to art, clothes and typography. And he might also have added: type designer, since in Gill Sans and Perpetua he designed two of the most popular faces of the century.

Eric Gill\* was born on 22 February 1882 in Brighton, the second child of a large family. He showed an early talent for drawing, and was sent to art school at Chichester, where the family had moved. At the age of sevcnteen his obvious interest in lettering and architecture led to his bcing apprenticed to W. H. Caroë, architect to the Ecclesiastical Commissioners in Westminster. At the same time he began to carve lettering, encouraged by W. R. Lethaby, the prineipal of the Central Sehool of Arts and Crafts, and very rapidly began to get commissions. At the Ccntral School he attended lcttering classes given by Edward Johnston, ten years his senior, and was profoundly influenccd by Johnston's integrity and approach to his work. In 1902 the two men shared rooms in Lincoln's Inn, until Johnston's marriage the next year, closely followed by Gill's the year after.

In 19o3 he left Caroë's office, where hc had never been content, and began his life-long career as a selfcmployed craftsman. He was immediatcly suecessful, and soon, besides carved work, he was commissioned by W. H. Smith to paint the sign on thcir bookshop in Paris, and several other branches thercafter, and by Count Harry Kessler to draw and engrave lettering for the title pages of books published by the Inscl Verlag in Lcipzig. The early lettering was drawn and reproduced by line blocks, but Gill was so dissatisfied with the result that for Die Odyssee (1910-I 1) he had the drawn lettering photographed on boxwood blocks, and cut the lettering by hand.

Gill's workshop in Hammersmith was proving too small, and with a young family to think of he moved in 1907 to the Sussex village of Ditchling. Here he began to carve sculpture, the medium in which some of his best work was done. His large publie commissions, such as the work for the BB on Broadcasting House in Portland Placc, London, made him nationally famous. Quite early on plans were made for him to work with Maillol and Epstcin; both came to nothing, for Gill was very much his own man, but they indicate the estcem in which he was held early in his career. At the same time, his spiritual progress led him towards the Roman Catholie church, and he and his wife were reccived in 1913. Shortly after this he was given the task of carving the Stations of the Cross in Wcstminster Cathedral, completed in 1918, his first major sculptural commission, because of which he was exeused army service during World War I.

Mcanwhile the community at Ditchling began to grow. Edward Johnston went to live there in 1912, and in 19 16 an aequaintanee of Gill's from Hammersmith, Hilary (Douglas) Pepler moved in and set up the St Dominie's Press, a small hand-press workshop for which much of Gill's carly engraving was done. But after a few years Gill quarrelled with Pepler, mostly over money: Pepler was a comparatively wealthy man and casual about business, whereas Gill had to support himself and his family, and was almost fanatically methodical about keeping time-shects and

Eric Gill (18821940) at Capel-y-ffin. Photograp y Howard Coster. National Portrait Gallery, London.

![](images/1ecefa2669df98ffacf0fdefb0d7578dd2ebec2e77e6473c5d46f7e04b7119e5.jpg)

accunts. In 1924, Gill moved hisgrown tue to a remote abandoned monastery in the Welsh mountains at Capel-y-ffin.

Shortly before he went, he had been asked by Stanley Morison to write about typography for The Fleuron, but had declined, saying that typography was 'not his country'. Morison and he had met about ten years before when Morison was working with Francis Meynell at the Meynell family publishing firm of Burns and Oates, and had commissioned Gill to engrave some initials for them. By November 1925, Gill had given in to Morison, and was drawing alphabets for him, alphabets which were, in the course of time, to become Perpetua.

Early in 1924, Morison had announced his hope that Monotype users would soon be able to add to their repertoire of classic revivals 'at least one original design' Much later he deseribed in A tally of types (1953) the way he came to ask Gill to make that design. He had found that the private press types were mostly too quaint. 'What was wanted was a design that, while being new, was of general utility and in no respeet unusual.' The calligraphers like Edward Johnston were trained to write letters that were formed freshly by the pen, with not enough of the modifications made by eenturies of expertise in cutting punches. 'The fine serif is not in origin calligraphic but epigraphic; not written but sculptured. It follows that a set of drawings of a finely serifed faee by a contemporary practitioner of lettering could best be made by one who was either an engraver on metal or wood; or, prefcrably, a sculptor

The Heroic Age is well typifed for us in Achilles, in the early doom that hangs over him, and the great renown

of generations it would last. The richest hoped it might last for ten. Our traditions say, in the second generation after Agamemnon the deluge came.

It came, the ancients said, in the form of the avenging Heraclids, come back to regain the heri-

on stone or slatc. On this analysis the problem became soluble, and Gill was the obvious man to solve it.'

In 1925 Morison, somewhat disenchanted with Monotype because of the mistake over Fournier, and also with a growing intercst in the technique of hand punchcutting, was becoming intcrested in the cutting of Frederic Warde's Arrighi italic by Georges Plumet. He had also been introduced by François Thibaudeau of Deberny & Peignot to Charles Malin (1883- 1955), a younger man than Plumet, who was to complete the cutting of Arrighi after Plumet's retirement. It was to Malin that Morison turned for his plan of turning Gill's drawings into type in the traditional way. This was partly also as a concession to Gill's initial reluctance to havc anything to do with machine production, and his condemnation of industrial design, in the generally accepted sense of the process whereby an 'artist' makes drawings for 'workmen' to carry out. Within a few years, as we shall see, Gill changed his mind completely, not without a good deal of self-justifying sophistry, and designed Gill Sans.

Years later, Morison wrote in A tally:

Long before the cutting of Perpetua, the Corporation had cut punches from new drawings made for the purpose by draughtsmen capable of making clear outlines. But as to a book-type of the highest ambition, no reproduction direct from the drawing-board had been as satisfactory as those made from type already existing, e.g. Bell's roman and italic. The difference between the drawn pattern and the engraved letter was crucial. Virtue went out with the hand-cutter when the mechanic came in with his pantograph and the rest of the gear. The new engineers were not what the old engravers were. They could mass-produce, or reproduce, punches; they could not create, or recreate, the engraved quality that had belonged to typography in the roman letter since 1465.'

This is a curious passage in the context of Perpetua, since Bell was cut several years after it was begun. It is even more curious in the context of Morison's rclations with Monotype, since some of the most successful faces he supervised there were recreations of types which certainly had cxisted but did so no longer. Moreover in the same book he had condemned Monotype Caslon, of which the foundry types had been available as models, as 'a complete failure as a facsimile'. Morison's whole work at

Monotype was dedieated to proving that the engineers eould produec good letters, given the right models in graphie form, an endeavour which culminated in his own Times New Roman, which was drawn by a non-specialist draftsman and had nevcr s  .Test pr  s is the most curious beeause in 1953 he did not believe it: eertainly when Van Krimpen made a similar point a few years later, Morison replied, 'I do not really believe that you mean that Monotype and foundry type are two essentially different things. I do not believe these differenees are esscntial. I believe they are different but I question whether the difference is so great.'

Yet there is no doubt that in 1926 Morison thought that Malin's work was a necessary preliminary to any eutting by Monotype. He did not doubt Monotype's ability to cut the type, but he did question their skill in transforming the drawings of a self-eonfessed amateur type designer into a coherent fount. He was eonfirmed in this opinion by Van Krimpen, whose Lutetia had been cut as a foundry type shortly before, and was reeut by Monotype some years later. Yet whereas Van Krimpen and his puneheutter Rädisch were expert partners in elose daily contact at

Monotype Perpetua (1929).

Enschedé, Gill, who had little knowledge of type design, was dealing with Malin at a distanee and with Morison as an intermediary.

In thc event, Malin's eontribution to Perpetua was almost forgotten. Beatriee Warde did not mention him by name in her artiele introdueing the type in The Fleuron 7, and Gill ignorcd him eompletely in his note on the type, giving all the eredit to Monotype: Thcse drawings were not made with speeial referenee to typography  they were simply letters, drawn with brush and ink. For the typographieal quality of the fount, as also for the remarkably fine and precise cutting of the punches, the Monotype Corporation is to be praised. In my opinion "Perpetua" is eommendable in that, in spite of many distinetive charaeters, it retains that commonplaceness and normality which is essential to a good book-type.'

Thc Paris foundry of Ribadeau Dumas cast a fount from Malin's punches, which was passed to Monotype in January 1927 for what became a long series of trials, since the sight of the cast and printed letters began to disturb Gill, who had up to then seen only smoke proofs. He objected to the blob on the tail of the y which Malin had introduced, found the g and the r too heavy, and agreed with Morison that the

capitals were too short; and the comments were passed on to the Monotype works to make the changes required. In spite of this, Perpetua exhibits in an extrcme form the tendency of thc Monotype works to draw letters with ruler and compasses. In a letter to Morison in November 1926 Gill drcw two diagrams which illustrated his contcntion that the uprights of roman letters should be imperceptibly 'waisted', and that the serifs should grow from them as though organically. Under his parody of the drawing office version with its parallel-sided uprights and mathematically circular brackets to the serif, Gill wrote, 'See how bloody thc above looks, and is.' If Morison passed this on to the works, they took no notice, as they hardly cver did, and as a result in the larger sizes of the Perpetua titlings, as in many Monotype faces, the point where the compasses take over from the ruler is quitc perceptible.

Drawings of decorated letters (1928), which later became Gill Floriated. By courtesy of the Monotype Corporation.

The type was delaycd both by attention to thc dctails of the roman, and by the italic. Gill was persuaded by Morison, as Van Krimpen was with Romulus a few years later, to substitute a sloped roman for the more usual cursive italic. (Slopcd romans keep thc roman lctter forms and incline them, more or less mechanically, whilc italics have a few different characters and terminal flicks rather than serifs.) Morison's belief was that as italics were originally intended for independent use, they should not be uscd for articulation in prose set in roman, a belief which was set out in his essay 'Towards an ideal italic' (The Fleuron 5, 1926). Felicity italic finally made its appearance in Beatrice Warde's article in The Fleuron 7 in 193o, which introduced the type; it looked rather like the later Joanna italic and was hardly ever seen again. A year later Gill made drawings of what became standard Perpetua italic, with a greater angle of slope and a few more cursive characters. As Morison confessed to Van Krimpen, The sloped roman idea does not go down so well in this office [Monotype] as it does outside. The reason for this is that when the doctrine was applied to Perpetua, we did not give cnough slope to it. When we added more slope, it seemed the fount required a little more cursive in it. The result was rather a compromisc.'

Perpetua, Series 239, was finally issued to the trade in 1932.\* For the specimen which appeared in the

## JOHNSTON

Ths broadshee is et n Johnston typeEdward Johnston (872-1944) was of the followers of William Morris (834-l896) who took a leading part in viingnterestod lettering erhe ecdencehelat-Vic fashions. In 1916 Johnston was commissioned by Frank Pick (1878-1941) to deign a special fount for the exclusive useof London's Underground and t asociated companies.The resulting Johnston sans-serif type was the forerunner of many sans-serif founts both in England and abroad, including that of Eric Gill (882-1940) who was Johnston's friend and pupil in this specialised field of design.Johnston is the standard type used for all official signs and notices throughout the London Transport system, and it is also used, where appropriate, for much of London Transport's general typographical publicty.

1930 Fleuron, Gill also drew a delightful decorated capital N. Morison asked him to design the complete alphabet for Monotype, but he declined: Robert Harling\* suggested he thought the design too freehand for the mechanical process of Monotype reproduetion. In 1936 he changed his mind, and these Floriated Initials were cut as Scries 431. For while Perpetua was making slow progress through the works, Gill was beginning to make typography very much his country. As carly as 1925, he had designed some bloek letters for the Army and Navy Stores, and used similar forms for signs at Capel-y-ffin to warn off uninvited visitors. Then in October 1926 he was asked by a young bookseller in Bristol called Douglas Cleverdon (who later published several books of his) to paint his bookshop name in these same capitals. While in Bristol he developed 'flu, and to pass the time in bed he drew some alphabets for Cleverdon to use as models for lettering in his shop, ineluding the sans-serif capitals. A few wecks later, Morison was staying with Cleverdon, and was interested in the alphabets. Earlier in the year he had tried to interest

# PUBLICITY AND SELLING CONGRESS

![](images/691457602d142cdd796857607c2ecab77193f3a71ec2ee34df84249fc1d0e78c.jpg)
