However, in 1923 there occurred his first major conversion, which made him renounce everything he had so far done. He visited the first big exhibition of the Bauhaus at Weimar.

The Bauhaus was established by Walter Gropius at the capital of the new German Republic in 1919, by combining two existing schools of art in the city. Gropius was an architect, a pupil of Peter Behrens, and had already designed a handful of pioneering buildings before his army service in World War I; but although the new school's chief purpose was to train architects and industrial designers to abandon their classical training and rethink the structure and function of everything from a house to a chair, the teaching was very broadly based. Many of the staff were painters: Lyonel Fcininger, Paul Klee and Wassily Kandinsky all taught therc, and the basic drawing and design courses were immcnsely influential. The typographical ideas which were soon put into practice in the Bauhaus's publications were given impetus by the work of artists and poets rather than printcrs, and particularly by the Russian Suprematists, the Constructivists, and the Dutch De Stijl group. The early Bauhaus prospectuses by Feininger and Itten were Expressionist jeux d'ésprit, but with the arrival in March 1923 of the Hungarian graphic artist Lazlo Moholy-Nagy (1895-1946), the Bauhaus style as it is remembered today was introduced: rigidly rectilinear, using heavy sans-serif types and rules. This was the style of the publicity material for the 1923 exhibition, which so impressed Jan Tschichold.

The Bauhaus typographers were fortunate that many German printers were already equipped with well-designed sans-serif types (as opposed to virtually no well-designed romans). Sans-serif faces had first appeared in England, in a Caslon foundry specimen book of 1816, and by the end of the century were widely used for publicity printing in Britain, the United States and Germany: even in the nineteenth century its no-frills appearance was taken as especially suitable for countries in the vanguard of the machine age. By the first decade of the twentieth century, all the major foundries had families of sans-serifs, sometimes called Grotesque (or just 'Grot') in Britain, always called Grotesk in Germany, and confusingly named Gothic in the United States, and

![](images/cdaa65a53570e17e6e04daeba254f64eaabdb8516beddfe108f89bf372b0049a.jpg)

![](images/616149072e6e9bad4285a067b76f1ceff48bbc21d9958340c220c810348796dd.jpg)

Antiquc in France. ATF issued Franklin, designed by Morris Benton (1904-13), Bauer the Venus family (1907-1927) and Berthold the Akzidenz/Standard group from 1896 on. There were many others, but it was the last two families that were mostly used by the Bauhaus typographers and then by Tschichold. What was ncw in their work was the almost exclusive use of a typc style which had up to then been uscd as one among many, and the explicitly ideological choice of sans-serif as a type 'of our time'.

Thc Bauhaus delighted many observers, but horrified many more, particularly locally. A change of statc governmcnt in Thuringia brought local hostility to a hcad, and in the spring of 1925 the Bauhaus moved to the more welcoming city of Dessau and the celebrated new building which Gropius designed for it. Here typography was taught by thc Austrian Herbcrt Bayer (born 19oo), who had been a pupil and assistant of Moholy-Nagy's. Baycr, following thc thcorics of Walter Porstmann's Sprache und Schrift (192o), now formulated the much disputed and reviled doctrine of setting text without capitals, which became school policy. Early Bauhaus displayed typography had bcen almost all in capitals; Bayer now took the theory one step further. His design for a singlc-alphabet type (1925) is distinctly inclegant, whilc Tschichold's bettcr looking one (1926-9) is ruined by its fidgety seagull e. Neithcr was cut. In one of the oddest cross-fertilisations in cultural history, a studcnt from thc Bauhaus went in the early 193os to work with Victor Hammcr in Florence, whose singlealphabet uncial types derivcd from a very different tradition. Hc did not stay vcry long.

Nor did thc Bauhaus. Gropius resigned as director in 1928 (together with Moholy-Nagy, Marcel Breuer and Bayer) and, after a brief interregnum, rccommendcd the architect Ludwig Mies van der Rohe as his succcssor; Mies dirccted the school during its final ycars, including a forccd movc to Berlin, until the Nazis closcd it down in 1933.

Its cffect was widcsprcad, though stamped out in the short term in Germany. Most of thc staff emigrated to thc Unitcd Statcs, Gropius via England, and in cnvironmental design we are still living with the results of their work. But in typography, the designcrs who made the Bauhaus widely known, cspecially Renner and Tschichold, wcre nevcr directly connectcd with it, but pursucd its aims from outsidc. Apart from Baycr's experimental alphabet, thc Bauhaus produced no type dcsigns. It was left to

# ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz

# ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz

others to create the simplified faces the new typographical style demanded.

Most of the nineteenth century English sans-serifs were jauntier, and retained more details from roman faces (such as the eycglass g), than the Venus and Akzidenz group, which took more seriously the implied constraints of block letters, that the strokes should always be of equal thickness. (Wherc strokes join, however, compromises are always made to counteract both the optical impression that converging strokes are thicker, and the danger of clogging with ink.) The German faces abolished the eyeglass g, and straightcned the tail of R, which was usually curved in the English types; they tidicd up many details, though they kept the doublc-bowled a and the curve in the tail of y, and chicfly the comfortable modern face proportions, in which the O is an oval, not a circlc.

The new generation of sans-scrifs, which began with the Ludwig & Mayer foundry's Erbar (1922-30), designed by Jakob Erbar, included Rudolf Koch's Kabel (1926-7), and culminated in Paul Renner's Futura (1928 on) for Bauer and the very similar Stempel design Neuzeit (1928), took the circular O as thcir proportional norm, as Edward Johnston's London Transport face had done a decade before. All straightened the tail of the y, though they varied in many other details. Futura and Neuzeit went furthest in geometrical purity, and had a single-bowled a.

In the final analysis, the most constructed sansserifs are vitiated by their own geometry, because the letters are isolated by it: they lack the sense of flow from one to the next which there is in the traditional letterforms and proportions. This onward movement is guided by details so subtle they go unnoticed, but they are the result of practical opties, not geometry.

After his first cxposure to the Bauhaus in 1923, Tschichold changed his own style completely. He Russianised his name temporarily to Ivan, to identify himself with the left-wing stance of the new movement, he adopted an uncompromising 'new typographical' manner, and in a special number of the printing tradc journal Typographische Mitteilungen in October 1925 hc eombativcly announecd his new ideas under the gencral title of 'elementare typographie' (all in lower-case on the cover), in company with Bayer, Moholy-Nagy, Kurt Sehwitters and the Russian El Lissitzky. He then followcd this up with a beautifully designed, rational yet radical, and above all practical serics of manuals: Die neue Typographie (the new typography', 1928), Eine Stunde Druck-

Jan Tschichold

gestaltung (a lesson in design for print', 1930), Schrifischreiben fir Setzer (lettering for compositors', 1931), and Typographische Entwurfstechnik (typographieal layout method', 1932). These made the new style not just a broadside against the bourgeoisie and blacklettcr, but a comprehensivc method eapable of day-to-day applieation.

Much of Tschiehold's writing was prompted by his activities as a teacher, for after a brief spell as a freelanee designer in Berlin he was invited by Paul Renner in 1926 to join thc Munich printing school of which Renncr was direetor. He stayed there until 1933, when he was arrested and interned by the Nazis, and his teaching contract cancelled. (Renner was dismissed, it will be remembered, and his place takcn by Georg Trump.) Tsehichold decided to leavc Germany, and moved to Basle, whcre he survived by teaching and some work for publishers; he beeame a Swiss eitizen during World War II.

Thc discipline of designing books on a variety of subjects, not just typographical manuals, for conservatively minded publishers on whom he depended for a livelihood, began Tsehichold's second, slower, conversion, to classicism. His style was becoming less doctrinaire before he left Munich, and two display

apologia which began by modifying his carlier pronouncemcnts against centred layouts, and came eventually to the extraordinary conclusion that the 'new typography', in its insistencc on ccrtain styles of type and dcsign, was inhcrently Fascist. Although they had little else in common, Tschichold was like Gill in being prone to rash, self-contradictory public utterances, but herc he excelled Gill.

Tschichold's new classical style was pcrfccted, and at the same time given its greatest scope, when aftcr World War II Allen Lane, thc founder of Pcnguin Books, invited him to come to England to restyle the Penguin list. Between thc beginning of 1947 and the end of 1949, he established composition rules for printers, overhauled the standard paperback covers, designed many series styles, and individually laid out a formidable number of books, notably the attractive hard-bound King Penguins, which were modelled on the Insel Verlag's little picture books, and for which hc had had some practice with the Birkhäuser classics in Switzerland. Tschichold's masterly use of subtly allusive typc and ornament combined within a strictly formal overall scheme has never been bettered.

After he returned to Switzerland, leaving Penguin design in the good care of Hans Schmoller, he continued to design, but his reforming zeal seems to have spent itself. But it is from this pcriod that his one significant typeface, Sabon (1966), dates. Its achievement was as much in what cannot be seen as in what can. In the early 196os a group of German printers decided they needed a type which could be set on Monotype or Linotype equipment, or in a foundry version by hand, with no perceptible diffcrcnce on the page. This mcant that all the drawbacks of both composing machines, the Monotype unit width grid and the Linotype's inability to kern, had to be resolved in a dcsign which should look, the specification said, like a Garamond made a whiskcr narrower for economy's sake.

Tschichold rose to the challenge with a professionalism which was astonishing in someone known bettcr as a user than as a creator of typefaces. His task was helped by all the manufacturing being done in

![](images/0c0215d602ea5ec1a39e169fc6b712829ea505be48b3640842559aa9873b567e.jpg)

Frankfurt, where Monotype had a factory (which had cut Dante a decade beforc), and where Stempel, who made thc foundry and Linotype versions, were based, but that does not diminish his success. Sabon is an admirable face, strong yet restraincd, with only a hint of Garamond about it. (It is called after Jacob Sabon, a punchcutter from Lyon, who is thought to have brought some of Garamond's matrices to Frankfurt.) The roman capitals in particular are so handsome that one regrets that no titling fount was produced, and that Tschichold never designed any classical display letters.

Jan Tschichold died on 1 1 August 1974, a designer who had excelled in several different styles at different times, and whose only fault was that he disregarded Bruce Roger's advicc about keeping his theories to himself.

# Berthold Wolpe

![](images/b5923779568e35ad8b366ad0d91a4960ed2c5d0bcf7d56a3de1c1bd8f13e61e6.jpg)  
Berthold Wolpe (born 1905). Photograph by Geoffrey Ireland.

Of the designers who studied with Rudolf Koch, Warren Chappell and Berthold Wolpe have produced the most interesting types. Chappell's Lydian has already been mentioned, but he also created Trajanus, issued by Stempel in 1939, a handsome pen-formed roman. Wolpe's work is more independent, and was conditioned by his training in metal work rather than calligraphy, and his types are sturdily individual designs.

Berthold Wolpe\* was born in Offenbach on 29 October 1905, the year before Koch began working at the Klingspor foundry there. In 1924 he went to the

Offenbach technical institute, where Koch had set up a workshop for students three years before; here he learned metal work, lettering, and later designed tapestries to be made in the workshop; and in 1929 he became a teacher in the school. He was involved in many collaborative ventures with Koch, notably the charming ABC Büchlein of 1934.

In 1932 he went to England, and finally made the decision to stay there in 1935. He worked for the Fanfare Press until 1939, and then from 1941 until his retirement in 1975 he designed jackets, bindings and some books for the London publishers Faber & Faber, where his hand lettering and bold layouts made their books instantly recognisable.

Wolpe had designed his first type in 1932; it was first called Matthias Claudius but later, because of the risk of confusion with Koch's Claudius, renamed Hyperion. It is a beautiful and unusual italic, for which punches were cut by Paul Koch in I 2D only. The Bauer foundry then produced a full range of sizes, and used the type in two delightful books of Wolpe's designs, Handwerkerzeichen in 1936 and Schmuckstücke und Marken in 1938; but the type appeared commercially only after World War II.

His next, and best known type, Albertus, was designed for Monotype in England and begun in 1932. The titling appeared first in 1935, the roman upper and lower case followed in 1938, and the light and the bold in 1939-40. Albertus was based on Wolpe's lettering cut in metal, which Stanley Morison had seen and liked so much he commissioned the face, and its origins survive clearly in the shapes of the letters. Wolpe himself has noted: 'On the bronze inscriptions the letters were not incised but raised; in other words the baekground was lowered and the outline only of the letters cut in. Such a metal inscription is cut with a chisel and not drawn with a pen, which gives it sharpness without spikiness, and as the outlines of the letters are cut from outside (and not from the inside outwards), this makes for bold simplicity nd euces th s to barei

In 1935, while he was working with the Fanfare Prcss, Wolpe designed Tempest for them, a vigorous sans-serif titling for use in display work, based on the freehand lettering he had developed for the jackets for the publisher Victor Gollancz, which Fanfare printed on the lines established by Morison. He also designed an attractive series of type ornaments for Fanfare about the same time.

In 1937, Monotype issued Wolpe's Sachscnwald, a bold calligraphic and simplificd blacklettcr. In the Koch tradition, although the blackletter capitals are alrcady considerably romanised for the English reader, Sachsenwald could be uscd with Albertus capitals, and was, in an edition of The Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam printed at Fanfarc in 1940.

Albertus (1938).

Pegasus, Wolpe's only other type issucd commercially, was designed for Monotype in 1937. Since it was cut on one size only it was unjustly neglected; but in the catalogue of a retrospcctive exhibition of Wolpe's work at the Victoria and Albert Museum in London in 198o, held to celebratc Wolpe's seventyfifth birthday, the type was adapted with thc help of Matthew Carter for composition on the Linotype VIP filmsetter; and with a new italic and bold by Wolpe, it

Wolpe has also designed a handsome set of floriated capitals for the Westerham Press, called Decorata (1955), and an italic to be used with Edward Johnston's London Transport sans-serif in 1973.

# Roger Excoffon

Roger Excoffon (191083) in 1960.Photograph by Roger Catherineau.

Roger Excoffon, like Dwiggins, was above all a talented graphic designer, who ran a flourishing advertising design ageney in Paris, ealled U&O, for many years. His posters for Air Franee and Bally shoes, done with a few economieal yet eloquent brush strokes, were brilliant. His types were designed primarily for advertising, and belong to a category neatly defined by the critic Gérard Blanchard as gestuel, for which the English equivalent 'gestural' does not quite convey the full flavour of an image flowing naturally from the hand and spirit. Nevertheless, Excoffon's masterpiece, the Antique Olive family, is both completely original and a perfect balanee between the gestuel and the typographic.

Excoffon was born in Marseilles in 1910, and such artistic skill he showed at school was chiefly in the drawing of maps. He went to university at Aix-en-Provenee to read law, but found his heart was not in it, and went to Paris to study painting. Instead, he turned inereasingly towards graphic art and letter forms. ln 1947 he formed his own agency, and also beeame design director of the small Marseille type foundry, the Fonderie Olive.

For them he had already begun to design an elegant stressed sans-serif called Chambord (1945- 5 1), whose capital alphabet was elearly modelled on that of A. M. Cassandre's Peignot (1937), drawn for the larger rival firm of Deberny & Peignot, but whose lower ease was a far more restrained, less quirky performance. He followed Chambord with Banco (195 1), a set of powerfully drawn sans-serif eapitals whieh look like strips of bent sheet metal, and three scripts: Mistral (1953), a vigorous flowing brush letter whieh manages the diffieult technieal feat of joining letters with insouciant skill; Choe (1953), another brush script like the jottings of a Chinese calligrapher; and Diane (1956), a joined eopperplate type whieh is the only serious rival to Zapf's Virtuosa in the field of formal seripts. The delightful Calypso (1958), aptly described by Blanchard as 'a game with orange

Left: Choc (1953), Mistral (1953), Diane (1956), Banco (1951) and Calypso (1958). Right: Vendôme (1952), designed by François Ganeau.

In his rôle as art director of the foundry, Excoffon guided the production of Vendôme (1952), whose nominal author was François Ganeau, a theatrical designer and friend of Marcel Olive, the foundry's president. Vendôme was a spirited  one is tempted to say, theatrical  rendering of Garamond, and was an extraordinarily popular type.

Then, between 1962 and 1966, Excoffon designed Antique Olive (Antique being the French term for sans-serif). Although this was clearly planned as a family of sans-serifs to rival Haas's Helvetica and Deberny & Peignot's Univers (both began to appear in 1957 and both were designed by Swiss), the French had designed no formal 'mechanical' sansserifs so far this century, and were not going to start now. Antique Olive was drawn with a subtlety normally reserved for classical romans and italics, and while the basic letter forms were in no way unusual, the shapes they took were totally new. Antique Olive was the most striking sans-serif since Futura and Gill, and is a more refined letter than either of them. We should remember Excoffon's analogy, quoted in the

# New types for newer machines

All the designers who have appcared so far saw thcir typcs first produced in metal, with the cxccption of Tschichold's few faces for the Uhertype photosettcr in the 1930s. The ncxt generation, those born in thc decades after World War I, were transitional, beginning their carcers designing metal type, then changing ovcr in midstream to drawing faces which would nevcr be cut and cast, but would go straight into film or later be digitised. What thesc changes involved, and how they affected type designers, is the subjec o this chapter.

Thc reader will remember that in 1922, the foundation of the Fleuron Socicty was suggested in London to show that mcchanical composition, which was then still considered a ncw tcchnology, could produce results comparable to those of traditional hand sctting. It came to nothing because of the disagrcement with this basic belief of Bernard Newdigatc (1869-1944), the manager of thc Shakcspcare Head Press, an influential typographical journalist, and a staunch believer in thc values of the arts and crafts movement and the private presses. Yet, in his bricf essay 'Respicc: prospicc' in the first numbcr of The Fleuron (1923), Newdigate rcfcrred to thc bright futurc of offsct lithography, which had been used by a Blackpool twicc-weekly newspaper for a year, and, whilc noting that the type foundries were cheerfully sccptical of any threatcncd cxtinction, to the advent of photocomposition, for which a patent had recently bccn taken out by Robcrtson, Brown and Orrcl. Newdigate concluded, 'Whether the quality of thc work is madc bctter or worse by the invention or devclopment of any ancilliary art or method depends on the way in which thc art or method is applied, rathcr than upon any inherent exccllence or dcfcct in the method itsclf. If thc method is uscd for cheap and nasty work, it docs not follow that thc mcthod itsclf is bad'. Thesc words arc just as apt now as they were then.

When Newdigate was writing, the technology of printing had bcen basically the same since Gutenberg: thc surfaces of metal letters cast in relief were inked and pressed against papcr. Thc way they were cast and assembled had changed, and the way pressure was applied, but not the general principlc.

An invention first made at the end of the eighteenth century was beginning to change this. The Bavarian Alois Senefelder found that a drawing madc with a greasy pencil on the smooth surface of a flat limestone slab could be damped, then inked, and printed: the water was repelled by the greasy image, and the ink by the water. The process was completely planographic, with no need for relief surfaccs, and was callcd lithography from the Greek words for stone and writing.

Throughout the nineteenth century, lithography was used for illustration, both in books, on posters and ephemera, and as artists' prints; and also for the printing of music. Already by the 1850s, photographs were successfully transfcrred to lithographic stones and printed. The process began to challenge letterpress for the printing of text only at the beginning of the twentieth century, when flexible metal plates werc introduced, and the image was transferred to the paper by way of a rubber blanket, which reduccd wear on thc plate and meant the plate image could be the right way round. This was called offsct lithography.

The advantages of offset were scvcral. Thc presses, being rotary (with both paper and plate on cylinders, as opposed to cylinder prcsscs wherc the typc was flat and thc paper cylinder rolled over it), could be run fastcr; the quality of the paper was less crucial for a good rcsult, and half-tone photographs could be run in with text without the need for shiny coated papers; and books could be stored for rcprinting as scts of plates or photographic film, and not as heavy, bulky pagcs of typc which lockcd up capital in expensive metal.

Until after World War II the general run of books printcd by ofet wee st on hot etal composig machines as though for letterpress, cleanly proofed and photographed on the lithographic plates; but the illogicality of this procedure led engineers from the end of the nineteenth century to devise ways of setting text by means of photography, with the lctters stored as master negatives, without the use of type at all. The English pioneer of cinematography, William Friesc-Grecne, patented a simple linc-composing machine in 1898, and over the next half-century many methods were tried out. The Uhertype system (1925), for which Jan Tschichold designed some alphabets, worked by means of a master character cylinder which rotated to select the correct letters according to the instructions of a perforated tape, and exposed them in sequence on a ribbon of lightsensitive film. In this it was typical of the first generation of filmsetters, which worked by the meehanical seleetion of the characters in positive or negative form, and photographic exposure of them on film or paper through lenses.

For example, the Monophoto, first announced in 1952, worked in principle much as the Monotype did: the tape from the keyboard unit was fed into the composing machine and determined the position of the diecase in a sequence of movements; but the diecase was made up not of matrices but of negatives of the characters, which were exposed on film or light-sensitive paper, with a lens unit dctermining the size of type.

In theory there should have been no visible difference bctween hot metal setting reproduced by offset, and filmset material in the same types, but in practice there was. The use of the same diecase for several sizes of type, while it had obvious economic advantages, meant that important optical adjustments in the type design according to its size were not made; the fit between letters was often imprecise at first; and thcre were variations in exposure and focus of the image. Corrections werc more difficult. Monotype composition is simple to correct by hand, but carly filmset corrections, however minor, had to be set lineby-line, and spliced in, and frequently the exposure of the correction line was heavier or lighter.

Other first generation filmsetters were the Lumitype and the Linofilm. The experiments of the French engineers Higonnet and Meyroud which led to the development of the Lumitype machine, called the Photon in the United States, began at the end of World War II, though the first production model was not installed until 1954, at the Patriot Ledger newspaper office in Massachusetts; while the Linofilm, previewed at the IPEX printing exhibition in 1955, was in action at the National Geographical magazine in 1959.

At the same time that photography and electronics were revolutionising character generation, the computer was beginning to help in the preparation of the information that was fed from the keyboard into the composing unit, both in the initial setting, and at the important correction stage. Firstly it helped with the justification of lines. On hot metal machines, the keyboard operator tapped out the line, using a variable space key for the gaps between words, while the machine registered on a simple scale the number of units of space consumed in each line. At the end of the line thc operator divided the number of remaining units by the number of variable spaces, and keyed in the width of each space.

Decisions on whether to split words, and where, depended on the good sense of the operator and the standards of the house; many typographers and master printers spent a great deal of effort in improving standards of composition, especially in insisting on close word spacing. Because good setting required thought, particularly over the question of breaking words, compositors who were ignorant, lazy or in a hurry tended to drive lines out, producing the loose word spacing which was and today remains all too common, especially in newspapers.

When computers were harnessed to the keyboards, they could make all the spacing and word-breaking choices, so that the operator simply tapped an endless line, known in the trade as 'idiot tape', and the machine did the rest. This obviously speeded up the keyboarding, though it made work dull for the opcrator; and because the line-breaking information could be added later, it also made it possible for decisions about type design and sizc, and measure, to be made, or changed, after keyboarding, which helped the designer. It also makes it possible in the end for authors to become thcir own keyboard opcrators, since as machincs become more compatible, disks produced on word-processors can be fed directly into typesetters, with no intermediate keyboarding stage. It is this prospect which has caused so much understandable unrest among the typesetting unions.

In theory, computers should have made bad setting impossible, because the result is no longer at the mercy of fallible compositors; in practice, of course, workable word-breaking programs have proved less partly at least bceausc programmers are fallible too.

The other great bencfit of eomputer-aided eomposition was in corrcetion. In hot metal setting, as we have seen, simple ehangcs were easily madc by hand on Monotypc composition, while whole slugs had to be recast on Linotype. Whiehever maehine was uscd, it was quite easy for new errors to be introdueed, and therc was, moreover, no reeord kept of the correetions in machine-readable form: the Linotype kept no reeord at all, and the Monotypc punched spool eould reproduee only thc original uneorreeted setting in case of disasters. Howcver, eomputer tapes or disks can be eontinuously correeted and updated, and as thc speeds of eomposing machines beeomes ever faster, it is easy for whole books to be eorrcetcd and run through again.

More recently, eomputers have eome to be used not only as an aid to keyboarding, but also in the aetual production of typefaces, both in composition and design. The newcr generations of eomposing machines, such as the Hell Digiset (1966), the RCA Videocomp (1967), the Linotron 505 (1969) and the Monotype Lasereomp 3000 (1976), did not set with fixed images of the eharacters, whether on grids or eireular bcarers, photographieally exposed in sequenee, but stored the shapes of the letters in digital form in their memories, and seanncd them, whole lines at a time, using cathode ray tubes or lasers.

In a letter to the tradc press in 1935, Erie Gill wrotc, 'My "Joanna" type was not designed to facilitate maehine punch eutting. Not at all. Maehines ean do praetically anything. The question isn't what they can but what they should.' Like many designers who left the fine tuning of thcir work to the manufaeturer's drawing office, Gill exaggerated the abilitics of maehines, which always have limitations. Similarly, the Freneh artist Roger Exeoffon took a lofty view of filmsetting, regarding type design as a philosophieal matter while typesetting methods werc a technicality which did not eoneern him. But these men are the exception: most designers havc always paid elose attention to these techniealitics.

Before the introduction of digital setting the technology of cold eomposition affeeted the look of typefaccs in two ways. The first was the faet already alluded to, that faees had to be designed so that many sizes could bc sct from one master, and the lcgibility adjustmcnts made to work for all of them. (Only the American Alphatype machine provided scparate masters for eaeh size, and even then several masters were made from one set of drawings, as with hot metal maehines.) The seeond was the ability to kern.

![](images/e9053df628c406d19ffac45ee8857aa29fd4ec6a0aa4b7d03c07dba9a776c653.jpg)

Type, sinee the invention of printing, had eonsisted of letters cast in relief on the end of a rectangular eolumn of metal; but it had often reprodueed the shapcs of more fluid manuseript letters. Italics and scripts always presented a problem to type founders beeause their inclination and ealligraphie flourishes tended to spread beyond the reetangles on whieh the eharaeters sat. Apart from a few script types whieh were east on lozenge-shapcd bodies, with triangular infills to even up the ends of the lines, such spreading eould bc aeeommodated only by easting the letters with projeeting parts, ealled kerns, whieh rested on the shoulders of the letters next door. Where the kerns were likely to hit parts of the adjaeent letters, as when an f was next to an i, an l, or another f, speeial doublc letters called ligatures had to be cut.

Whcn type was east by machine, the Monotype could make kerned eharacters without diffieulty, but slug machines like the Linotype, beeause they assemblcd all the matrices in a row before the line was cast, could not. The Linotype drawing office therefore had to restructure every character which might project outside its body, producing, particularly in italics, a characteristically crabbed f and y. In the English Linotype Granjon (1924), designed by George W. Jones to compete with Monotype Garamond, the flourish of the roman f, usually bent back in Linotype faces, was kept by including no less than twenty-six ligaturcs, which were intended to cover all eventualities. When Beatrice Warde, the Monotype publicity manager, reviewed the typc, even while commending its beauty she could not resist pointing out that as there was no fg ligaturc it could not set 'Afghanistan'. (It could not manage 'Kafka' either, but the writer was not yet well known, and neither could the Monotype.) The only slug caster which could produce italic letters of a kerned appearance was, as we have seen, the Ludlow, which adopted the lozenge-shaped script method, and had inclined matrices for some faces.

With photosetting, the problem vanished, and letters could overlap the notional rcctangle occupied by their neighbours without trouble. They could even join up, and script types like Matthew Carter's Snell Roundhand for the Linofilm (1966) could be de-

## ITALIC

It can be considered a special merit creative forces are again concerned of type design - a problem which has the best artists of every age. Type and always been the immediate expression signed like copperplate. Also, because there was no longer any technical reason for ligatures, they have tended to disappear as an extravagance, though their Renaissance elegance has been missed by many, and the overlaps which replaced them are often clumsily handled.

![](images/f58cfa5b711127641b99639c44f26de393ddb2e91965b74e08cc33b64c19ceda.jpg)

When type occupied metal rectangles, the fit and relationship of letters was established by type founders and composing machine manufacturers. Capitals could be spaced out and even, by careful trimming, closed up in individual cases, but thc space between lower case letters was in general fixed. But filmset type is far more flexiblc, and can be set with more or less space between the letters. Such freedom is dangerous in the hands of users with no sense of the natural rhythm of a typeface, and they unfortunately seem at present to be in the majority. On the bonus side, spacing programs have been developed for what is known as 'multi-sector kerning', which can adjust the optical space between pairs oflower-case characters with a degree of attention that in the days of metal typc was given only to capitals.

In a digital typeface, the image is composed of a grid of squarcs, each identified by a coordinate. The typesetter opcrates like an extrcmely complicated and extremely fast game of 'battleships and eruisers', building up the letters square by square in a series of sweeps. A eurve is therefore deseribed not purely, but as a suecession of steps, and this will be more or less apparent aceording to the fineness of the grid. In a coarse resolution they are quite visible to the naked eye. Designers have therefore had to devise letter forms whieh best survive this kind of eoarsening, in whieh subtle eurves play no part and the details resist distortion, whether it be enlargement leading to elogging, or reduetion leading to disappearanee. As an extreme example of what ean be done, the San Franeisco team of Charles Bigelow and Kris Holmes have developed Lucida, a face for laser printers in offiee use, in which the resolution is very coarse (between 240 and 480 dots per ineh, about a quarter of the definition of most digital typesetters for the printing industry): nevertheless, Lueida remains graeeful, and the italie is partieularly attraetive.

The other way eomputers have come to be used is in the aetual design of type. The first use was in developing different weights from a basie face, the kind of work whieh absorbed most of Morris Benton's working life. Using Dr Peter Karow's Ikarus program, the outline of the faee ean be plotted and fed into the eomputer, and then rearranged aeeording to the weight required, as wcll as sloped and otherwise played about with. But other programs have been developed whereby it is possible to draw faees on a sereen and fit them for eomposition automatieally. As one designer has put it, it is now possible to design a typeface before luneh and be setting it during the afternoon.

Sueh new freedom has meant a new kind of relationship between design and produetion. In the carly days of filmsetting, the development of new machines was done mainly by the big manufaeturers, Linotype and Monotype, and a few foundries, such as Deberny & Peignot and Berthold; but new firms rapidly joined in. One common faetor was that advanees in engineering quiekly outstripped the ability of the drawing offiees to keep up: for while it was relatively easy to adapt existing faces for the new maehines, doing it well took longer, and the kind of typographie subtleties whieh had become commonplaee in the days of hot metal were often forgotten. The fitting of letters, their relationship with eaeh other, beeame notoriously erratie until digital typesetting anehored the letters firmly again.

It was partly to stop this abuse that the Association Typographique Internationale (usually abbreviated to A.Typ.I) was set up in 1957 under the presideney of Charles Peignot of Paris, and later John Dreyfus, to work towards an international eonvention on typeface proteetion. An agreement was reaehed in 1973 at the Vienna Congress on Industrial Property, when ten countries signed an Arrangement for the Proteetion of Typefaces; but unfortunately ratifieation of this agreement still awaits the necessary modifieation of the signatory eountries' internal laws.

## Banks's Artists and his Endeavour Collections

matrix disks for the Lumitype machine, and thcn designed Méridien and Univers for both the Lumitype and for foundry metal type for hand setting. Univers was quiekly recognised as an important facc by John Dreyfus at Monotype, and cut for hot metal composition, and also put in the Monophoto repertoire, and in 1962 Dreyfus commissioned Frutiger directly to design Monotype's first face freshly conceived exclusively for film composition, Apollo. Monotype followed this with Albertina (1 964) by the Dutchman Chris Brand, and with Photina (1971), by José Mendoza y Almeida.

Mendoza is a French designer of Spanish descent who had to abandon his typographical training because of the early death of his father in 1944. After an apprentieeship with the photo-engraving firm of Cliehés Union in Paris, he was introdueed by Maximilien Vox to Roger Excoffon, and worked from 1954 t0 1959 as his assistant at the Olive foundry, after which he beeame a freelance. In 1959 the Amsterdam foundry issued his Pascal, a most attraetive stressed sans-serif.

Photina was the most original and successful of the Monophoto designs, with a particularly interesting italic, frcely designed yet disciplined by Mendoza's skill in cutting lettering in wood.

Two more recent Monophoto faces have been a variation on the Mécane theme, Margaret Calvert's Calvert, and on Century, Robin Nicholas's Nimrod, both in 1980.

Because of the particularly resolute adherence of the English newspaper industry to hot metal setting, particularly in Fleet Street, English Linotype undcr the direction of Walter Tracy went on producing designs for metal types into thc 197os, as we have seen in an carlier chaptcr. In thc United States, however, books werc more commonly set on Linotype machines and ncwspapers were converted earlier to filmsetting. Mergcnthaler Linotype, where C. H. Griffith's position was takcn by Mike Parker, undertook an advcnturous policy of design for film, and in 1965 Matthew Carter was brought in as designer.

Matthew Carter was born in England in 1937, the son of Harry Cartcr, the printing historian. In 1955 he went to study at the Haarlem firm of Enschedé, where he learned punehcutting from Van Krimpen's assistant P. H. Rädisch. He returned to England in 1957, and after some freelanee work in London went to design typefaees for the Lumitype at Crosfield Eleetronics, the English distributor of the machine, and then joined Mergenthaler Linotype in New York.

His early work there was largely in drawing scripts, in which, as we have secn, Linotype had some catching up to do now that filmsetting made kerning possible. Snell Roundhand (1966) was based on a seventcenth century copperplate script, and used to the full the Linofilm's ability to overlap characters. In the same year he produced the more conventional brush drawn Cascade Script, and in 1970 Gando Ronde, partauthored with Hans-Jörg Hunziker. In the same year came Auriga, a Mécane with some subtle details of interest rather than beauty, and he began work on the handsome Olympian, a newspaper face which has already becn referred to. In 1977 he produced Vidco, an uncomplicated sans-serif.

In the middle 197os Mike Parker and Carter turned their attention to the fresh design of an old style facc for film composition, and decided to base it on the types of the French punchcutter Robert Granjon (1513-89), which had already been the starting point of two classic types of the twenticth century, Monotype Plantin in 1913 and Times New Roman in 1932. Carter's Galliard, which appeared in 1978, is, as its name suggests, a far more dashing interpretation of the Granjon letter, based on no particular size but, as Carter put it, 'an anthology'.

Granjon was celebrated especially for his italics, and Carter's italic was drawn with particular verve.

In the same year as Galliard, Carter designed Bell Centennial, as described in the Introduetion to this book, and more recently both hc and Parker have left Linotype to form Bitstrcam, a design agency in Cambridge, Massachusetts, concentrating on the digitisation of typefaces for laser printers and other newer ways of generating visible language.

The other established manufacturer to take seriously the challenge of filmsetting was the Berlin foundry of H. Berthold AG. Founded in 1858, it first showed its Diatype photosetter at the DRUPA printing exhibition in its centenary ycar; over the suecceding years it introduced modifications and new models, and took over Alphatype in 1981. It also adopted an enterprising design policy under its

type director Gunter Gerhard Lange (born 1921), appointed in 1973. Lange was an original designer whose Solemnis (1952) has already bcen referred to, and whosc Concord was issucd by Berthold in 1969; and also a sophisticatcd adaptor of classic faccs such as Baskerville, Caslon, Garamond and Walbaum. In 1976 Berthold produced Hermann Zapf's Comenius, the most handsomc of thc batch of film faces that Zapf, whosc work is described in a later section, based on his metal Hunt Roman.

At the same time as the oldcr firms were adjusting to the changed conditions, newer organisations were appearing to markct filmsetters and CRT machines. Not all had any pretensions to typographical distinction, but among those who did was the Swiss company Bobst Graphic, set up in 1972 and ten ycars later absorbed by Autologic of Lausanne. Several of their faces, Media, Signa and Haas Unica, were designed by the team of André Gurtler, Christian Mengelt and Erich Gschwind, and among the others were Robert Norton's Else, and Bram de Docs's Trinité, which both appeared in 1982.

De Does occupies the position at Enschedé formerly held by Jan van Krimpen, and his predccessor's influence is detectable, though not

The commission for Trinité bcgan in 1978 with a request from Bobst to Enschedé for permission to adapt Van Krimpen's Romanée for filmsetting; De Docs's advice was that since the conversion of a design from metal to film was just as complicated a matter as designing afresh (an opinion which Van Krimpen would have endorsed, as we havc seen), the latter option was preferable. As a result he was asked to design Trinit as a joint commission from Enschedé and Bobst. The name rcflected the unusual feature of the type, that there were three alternative lcngths of extenders. Long extenders were earlier supplicd on dcmand for some Monotype faces, but it was unprecedented for a typc to come equipped with three such standard versions. There were also two widths of roman and italic, a medium and a bold. Trinité is a handsome and original face in the tradition of excellence associated with Enschedé, and while the italic seems to lack a certain degree of bite, the roman is admirable.

The other new manufacturcr to produce faces of distinction was the firm of Rudolf Hell of Kiel in Germany, who developed the Digiset machine in the middle 196os. For them Hermann Zapf produced four types between 1976 and 1983, to be described in the scction dcvoted to his work; and since 1978, the Dutch designcr Gerard Unger has produccd a series of excellent faces. Demos (1978), Praxis (1979) and Flora (1980) were conecived as a eombinable family, Dcmos being a squarely drawn workhorsc roman and italic, and Praxis a sans-scrif vcrsion of thc samc skcleton letter. Flora can bc used as the italic for Praxis or independently, and although its idea sprang from Sehneidler's Grafik (1934), it is by far the most original face of the three. In it the sans-serif form eomes as close to a true eursive as is possible. Although its strokes arc vertieal, it givcs an extraordinary effeet of movement and calligraphic flow, and is one of the most interesting, individual and attractive faccs to have appearcd rcccntly.

Novarese was born in 1920, and the majority of his types were designed for the Turin foundry of Nebiolo. The earliest and best known were produced in partnership with Alessandro Butti (1893-1959), notably the classical titling Augustea (195 1) and the square sans-serif eapital fount Microgramma (1952), to which Novarese later added a lower case and called it Eurostile (1962). Like Middleton at Ludlow, Novarese has been responsible for the general quality of Nebiolo's faces, producing Egyptians, scripts, a Bodoni, and many others, including Garaldus (1956), one of the only types called after the Vox Classification.

Apart from typewriter setters such as the IBM Selcctric, which carried imitations of popular typefaces on their golf balls, the other development which has greatly influenced the look of print has been the growth of dry transfer lettcring. Before thc arrival of Letraset in 1961, all displayed mattcr for line-block or offset reproduction had to be set on a photolettering headliner, pulled as a reproduction proof from metal type, or drawn by hand. This made life difficult for small-scale users who had no typesetting equipment and were not calligraphers. The ability to buy sheets of letters which could be easily rubbed down transformed their lives, as well as the work of countless amateurs who had to produce posters for village fêtes and jumble sales. Dry transfer lettering is far from idiot proof, but used properly it has been immensely beneficial.

In the design of typefaces it has in general followed the composing machine manufacturers, and the relative cheapness of production has meant that the lunatic fringe of the market has been fairly visible. But some of the designs of Colin Brignall for Letraset, notably Aachen (1969) and Romic (1979) have been reissued by Linotype.

This brief survey of the post-war explosion of typesetting methods and some of the typefaces produced for the new machines, has referred on occasions to the two leading type designers of the period, Hermann Zapf and Adrian Frutiger. Their careers, which straddled the ehanges from metal type to film and digitisation, will now be more fully described in the next chapter.

![](images/89a5884c0c7d2425096dd640ac8793f01242ba49c91ba47ce0619a827fa2b50b.jpg)

At the cnd of World War II, German printers and type founders had lost a high proportion of thcir plant and typographical material as thc result of bombing, and faccd the formidable task, along with most of industry, of rebuilding and re-cquipping their factories. Morcover, thcy had to do so almost cxclusively with roman faccs, since one of thc few useful legacics of the Third Rcich was the adoption of roman as the standard alphabet, or Normalschrift , for German, in place of blackletter. The decision to change had been taken in 1941; beforc thcn blackletter had been promoted by the Nazis with considerable cffort as the quintessentially German script, and afterwards was condemned as a Jewish invention.

Those printers who before the war had used roman types the most had bcen the book houses, largely concentrated in Lcipzig and thereforc now in the castern zone. The foundrics of the Federal Republic had to work almost from scratch, and sought designs for the new romans and italics that were necded. Chief among those who camc forward to provide them was the young Hermann Zapf.\*

Zapf was born on 8 November 1918 in Nuremberg, the birthplacc, almost exactly forty-two ycars earlier, of Rudolf Koch. He left school (wherc his reports gave him a B in penmanship) in 1933. In that ycar his father, who as a trade union official in a large car factory had bccome, with thc rise of the Nazis, politically undesirable, was sacked from his job, and as a result Zapf was debarred from further cducation and therefore the carcer in cleetrical cngincering hc had hopcd for. Instcad, he bcgan a four ycar apprenticeship as a retoucher at a local printing firm, Karl Ulrich & Co.

In 1935 an cxhibition of the work of Rudolf Koch, who had dicd the previous year, was shown in

Nuremberg, whieh inspired Zapf to become a calligrapher. He bought Koch's instruction book Das Schreiben als Kunstfertigkeit ("the skill of calligraphy'), and Edward Johnston's Writing and illuminating and lettering, and began a course of self improvement which resulted in his beeoming one of the great modern masters of the art. His apprenticeship with Ulrieh ended in 1938, and he went to work at Paul Koch's workshop at the Haus zum Fürsteneek in Frankfurt, where he began to learn something of the eraft of printing with the hand press, as well as puncheutting and related skills. In the autumn he was introduced by the printing historian Gustav Mori, whose legendary library he was able to use, to the Stempel foundry in Frankfurt, for whom he designed many of his best types. Here he met the superlative puncheutter August Rosenberger (1893-198o), with whom he collaborated on several magnificent books.

Early the following year Zapf began work on his first type for Stempel, a Fraktur blackletter called Gilgengart. As he had to learn as he went along, the type developed slowly, and suffered further from the disfavour into which blackletter fell after 194 1. As Zapf wrote later, 'A new printing type has a long, often thorny way to completion. Before a type has come far enough to please outsiders, it adds grey hairs to its co-producers.' Gilgengart was cut in two forms, the second narrowing the capitals of the first, and also omitting some decorative flicks on the bottom of the letters.

Concurrently with Gilgengart, Zapf designed for the Bärenreiter-Verlag of Kassel a music type, Alkor, which was destroyed during the war, and began work on his early masterpiece of book production, Feder und Stichel ('Pen and graver'), a book of twenty-five calligraphie alphabets. The drawings were finished in 1941, and the plates hand eut in lead by August Rosenberger in his spare time. The book first appeared as a limited edition from Stempel's own private press in 1949.

In 1941, Zapf was called up, and served as an army eartographer in France, first in Dijon and later Bordeaux, where he began a set of flower drawings whieh were used in Das Blunen-ABC (flower alphabet'), again cut by Rosenberger and published by the Stempel press in 1948. Iis war serviee ended as technieally a French prisoner in various military hospitals, but he was quickly released.

On his return he took up the design direction of Stempel, and began the enormous task of rebuilding

its repertoire of faces after the devastation of the war. Already in 1946 he had begun work on a pen-formcd roman called Novalis, which was not dissimilar to Warren Chappell's pre-war Trajanus for the same foundry, but was never issued. This work was quickly overtaken by a new face based on Renaissancc forms for which the first drawings were ready in 1948, and which made its first appearance in the introduction to Feder und Stichel the following year. It was called Palatino.

Palatino has proved so universally popular that its remarkable qualities tend to be taken for granted. Zapf himself wrote in About alphabets,\* "The studies and sketches of my Italian visit were converted into the Palatino type family, thcir very names suggcsting their Italian modcls', and ccrtainly the type's classical sensc of proportion shows evcry sign of the powerful impact made by the experiencc of his first visit to Italy on an impressionable young man who had hitherto been working in the Koch tradition. Palatino is so completely unlike such characteristic pre-war German roman types as Weiss Antiqua and Koch's Marathon that the direct influence of Italian lettering seems needed to explain it. Ncvertheless, the fact is that Zapf did not go to Italy until the Autumn of 1 95o, by which time Palatino and Michelangelo, those miraclcs of Renaissance grace, were already cut. Only Sistina, the more robust titling fount, was based on sketches actually madc in Italy.

Together with this extraordinary and apparently instinctive absorption of classical letter forms, Palatino still shows beneath its surface the stirrings of penmanship learned from Koch, which leaves small but vital traces on the contours of the letters. While this is never allowed to dominate the shapes, it givcs the type its crispness, and prevents it ever becoming bland. The earliest cuttings were more calligraphic in some details, such as the absencc of serifs on thc tails of p and q, but these were added later.

Palatino was immediatcly adapted for Linotype composition, since Stempel had held the contract for making matrices for the German branch of Mergenthaler Linotype since 19oo, and it has since been adapted for virtually cvery typesetting system in the West. As it was found somewhat heavy in wcight for text setting in the smaller sizes, Zapf designed a ligterversion, calle Aldus, for Linotyp compostion sizes (1952-3).

At the same time as Palatino, Zapf was at work on a ncwspaper facc, called Melior, a modern and sophisticated addition to the lonic family with the curves drawn on a principle which foreshadows the superellipse, a mathematical construction described fifteen ycars later in Scientific American. At the same time, the detuils of Mclior were carefully designed to withstand as far as possible the various processes, rotary letterpress, offset and gravure, which would usc it, and Zapf was able to study the technical requirements of such types at Linotype's Brooklyn headquarters during his first visit to the United States in 1951.

Melior appeared in 1952, and its originality can be gauged by comparing it with Zapf's far more conventional Mergenthaler Antiqua of two years later, which was intended for specialist scientific work, and presented technical rather than aesthetic challenges.

We can get an idea of the amount of work Zapf produced in these ycars when we realise that between 1948 and 1954 he designed not only Palatino, Aldus and Melior, with their titlings and bolds, italics and swashes, but also many other faces. There were the elegant Virtuosa scripts (1952-3), and several Grcek alphabets, some made to accompany existing Stcmpel faccs such as the sans-serif Neuzeit, but including the beautiful Phidias (1953) to partner Michelangelo, Frederika (1953) to go with Virtuosa, and the exccllent all purposc Heraklit (1954). Hc drew the Mergenthaler Antiqua alrcady mentioned, as well as handling the drawings for the German Linotype Janson (1952), based on the Stempel Janson types (which have already made their appearance in conncction with Monotype Ehrhardt), and for Stempel's cutting of a missing 24pt and a new 48pt. He designed the attractive Saphir decorated capitals (1952) and the vigorous jobbing face Kompakt, and a successful Persian script as well as many ornaments. And as if this werc not enough, he began work on a new type which at its devclopment stage was called significantly Neu-Antiqua ("new roman'), but was later christened, as a linguistically logical successor to Mclior, Optima.

Optima is in many ways Zapf's most original design, in that it tablishe virtually on its own a new type category, which its creator was careful to call a 'scrifless roman', to distinguish it from stressed sansserif. He tells us that it was inspired by Florentinc lettering he had seen on his Italian visit, which

dispensed with serifs; it is also more originally an extension of the development of roman book types, taking the comfortably round proportions of a transitional faee sueh as Baskerville's and shedding the serifs with no distortion of its essential nature and no sense of anything missing.

Although Optima was not originally eoneeived as a book face, beeause of the widespread opposition to sans-serif for text setting, Zapf's conversations with Monroe Wheeler of the New York Museum of Modern Art eonvineed him that its roman infleetions made it legible enough for eontinuous reading. Time has proved Wheeler right. Optima appeared at the DRUPA printing trade fair at Düsseldorf in 1958, and has been extremely popular ever sinee. A serifless italie defies imagination, and so in eommon with most sans-serifs a sloped version was produeed to aeeompany the roman. Various bolds followed, with the boldest forms growing vestigial serifs on some letters to help with their artieulation.

The logie of produeing Optima at this time was eonfirmed by the almost eontemporary history of Zapf's 'straight' sans-serif, Magnus, designed without much enthusiasm for English Linotype, of whieh the trial size was eut in 1957. The projeet was abandoned in 1960 after the appearance of Adrian Frutiger's Univers.

At the end of 1956, Zapf resigned from the type direetorship of Stempel, although he kept very elose links with the foundry. The administrative burden was threatening to outweigh his ereative work, and he wanted to develop his talents in book design, ealligraphy and other graphie teehniques. The portfolios and books of typographieal design and lettering he produeed over the years have been outstanding. Beginning with Feder und Stichel, already deseribed, and eontinuing with the magnifieent landseape-format Manuale typographicun of 1954, Typographische Variatiouen of 1963, the seeond upright volume of the Mauuale in 1968, and the privately issued Orbis typographicus of 198o, the books are an extraordinary outpouring of graphie ereativity.

It is unfortunate that the projeeted Manuale phototypographicuu of the late 196os was abandoned for finaneial reasons: the first two Mauuale volumes were set in metal, the first by hand and the seeond with some additional meehnieal eomposition, and it would have been suitable to produee a third with photosetting to represent Zapf's eontinuing and passionate involvement in the developing teehnology of type

composition. For although he has continued to value metal type and design for it, he has at the same time both overseen the transfer of his faces to film and digitisation, and also given his attention to designing for the new typesetting systcms.

Nevertheless, threc of Zapf's best designs for cold composition, Comenius for Berthold, Zapf Book for the International Typeface Corporation, and Marconi for Hcll Digiset, all dating from 1976, are children of what must bc one of the last instances of a typc commissioned for private usc and cast in mctal by a commercial foundry. In 196o, hc was invited as visiting professor of design to the Carnegic Institute of Technology at Pittsburgh, and the following ycar was commissioned by the Hunt Botanical Library there to design a face for use in the Library's publications, chiefly as a display letter to use with their standard text typc, Monotype Spectrum. He developcd his design from a pcn letter he had occasionally used, in which the stress was almost vcrtical, and drew an alphabet which was transitional in origin but of its time in appearance. Hunt Roman was intendcd for hand setting only, and so Zapf was able for oncc to dcsign without having to worry about the unit systems of mechanical composition, and because it was to be used in the Unitcd States it did not have to conform to thc standard base-line of German types. With this freedom he produced a beautiful facc, with enough calligraphic crispness to make it lively, and enough strength to discipline that liveliness. It was cut in four sizes by Stcmpel, and thc 18pt pilot size was completcd in May 1962.\*

The only disadvantages of Hunt werc that it had no italic, and was unavailable to thc tradc. Comenius, Zapf Book, and Marconi, of fourtecn years later, wcre by no means exact adaptations, but thcy bear a close family resemblancc, and both madc good these deficicncies. Comenius is the supcrior vcrsion, but all lack the sharpness of Hunt, possibly in unconscious recognition of the softness of the medium for which they wcre designed, by contrast with metal. In Zapf International, which ITC brought out the next year, 1977, the absence of sharp-edged features was actually requircd in its design brief.

One other typeface for ITC should be noticed: the popular italic Zapf Chancery (1979), which turned the arguments about italie versus sloped roman by providing a slightly inelined italie in place of 'roman', and an italie of greater inelination for emphasis. As Kris Holmes\* has pointed out, Zapf Chaneery has distinetly more bite, almost a touch of blackletter, by comparison with most faees for cold composition (ineluding Zapf's two carlier designs for ITC), though if, as she says, tongue-in-eheek, the x 'has the vigour of Gene Kelly in his prime', Gene Kelly is about to fall over. Zapf Chaneery's first extended showing was in a catalogue designed by Carl Zahn for the Boston Museum of Fine Art, but its chief rôle is elearly as an advertising display face.

In 1966, Zapf was appointed a typographical consultant to Hallmark International, the makers of greetings cards. Here he devised training sehemes and manuals for their lettering eraftsmen, eollaborated on an edueational film ealled The art of Hermann Zapf (1968), and also designed a handful of proprietory faces, covering most of the stylistie range from scripts to roman. Between 1967 and 1972, there appeared at roughly annual intervals: Jeanette, a script, Firenze, a chaneery italic, Textura, a blackletter, Hallmark

Comenius (1976). Kris Holmes, Zapf Chancery', Fine priut, 6, 1, 1980.

Uncial, Missouri, a ealligraphie roman, Seriptura, another seript, and, also in 1972, Crown, a roman book type for use in Hallmark's own Crown Editions. The types eould also be used in combinations: for example, Winehester used Uneial Capitals with Textura lower case, and Charlemagne mixed Uneial capitals with lower case from the Shakespeare designed by Zapf's wife Gudrun Zapf-von Hesse.

After leaving Stempel in 1956, Zapf also beeame a consultant to Mergenthaler Linotype in New York. For their Linofilm typesetter he designed in 1969 the seript Venture and the italie Medici. (The latter was also the name of Palatino during the development stage, but the two types should not be eonfused.) At the end of his advisory term in 1974, he drew Orion, a deliberately neutral text face.

For the Digiset digital typesetter manufactured by the Rudolf Hell company of Kiel, he has designed Marconi (1 976), another variation on the Hunt theme done in the same year as Comenius and Zapf Book, Edison (1978), a chunkier workhorse type for newspapers and teehnical publieations, Vario (1982) and Aurelia (1983). (Aurelia was also the provisional name for Sistina, and as with Mediei the two should not be confused.)

Marconi Text, Marconi kursiv, Marconi halbfett und Marconi halbfett kursiv  eine neue Digiset-Schrift im klassizistischen Stil von Hermann Zapf. Die Marconi hat ein groes Schriftbild mit einer hohen Mittellänge. Die Strichstärke fur den Grundschnitt wurde ebenso wie die Serifen krätig gewählt. Um die Schrift etwas weicher und ausdrucksvoller zu machen, wurden die Schnittpunkte von Senkrechten und Serifen leicht gerundet und die Schrägen etwas verlaufend ausgeführt. Marconi (1976).

Zapf's lifclong concern with non-roman types has continued, with Greek and Cyrillic versions of many of the faces so far mentioned. For the University of Wisconsin, at the suggestion of Walter Hamady, he produced a Sequoia alphabet (1984-7), and in 1983 he began to work on a roman-based Pan-Nigerian type to accommodate the nine chief languages of that country.

In 1977, Zapf was appointed Professor of Typographic Computer Programs at thc Rochester Institute of Tcchnology in New York State, and in 1983 his work with the Metafont design program developcd by Professor Donald Knuth at Stanford University produced the Euler type family for the American Mathematical Society. The basic roman is, like Zapf Chancery, really an upright italic, with rounded contours rathcr in the manner of Goudy; and there are script, italic and even Fraktur versions, as well as many mathematical sorts. At the samc time as this design, drawn on a screen without the aid of cven a pencil and paper, let alone a file, Zapf has recently returned to the original technique of making type, and designed a Civilité script for which the punches were cut by hand by Paul Hayden Duensing and cast at his private foundry at Kalamazoo, Michigan.

Thus Zapf's first ambition to become an electronic engineer has been realised by the most round-about route imaginable. Unquestionably we are the richer that the political barriers put up against his early training in electronics forced him into printing, where he cxpericnced, compressed into one career, the whole history of type manufacture so far, from hand punchcutting at the Haus zum Fürsteneck, through the industrialised foundry at Stempel, mechanical and film composition at Linotype, to the computerised design and character generation of today. We are doubly fortunate that he had both the technical ability to make the new machines work for him, instead of relinquishing control to the engineers, and also the graphic genius to make them work to such advantage.

Diotima (1952) and Smaragd (1953).

The early editions of Zapf's Das Blumen-ABC, Feder und Stichel and Manuale typographicum, issued in the late 1940s and early 1950s, were bcautifully bound by a young Frankfurt binder called Gudrun von Hesse (born 1918), who had learned bookbinding with Otto Dorfner in Weimar. In 1935 she began to teach herself lettering using the Edward Johnston and Rudolf Koch manuals which were an inspiration to so many calligraphers, and in 1941 studied in Berlin with Johannes Boehland, the designer of the spirited

brush seript Balzac (1951) for Stempel. In 1952, Stempel issued her beautiful Diotima roman and italie, which had been tried out in the wedding invitation for her marriage to Hermann Zapf the previous year. (He jokingly referred to this as trying to neutralise the opposition.) Gudrun Zapf later added to the Diotima family an open titling alphabet, Smaragd (1953), and a set of swash italic capitals, Ariadne (1954).

In 1968 shc designed Shakespcare roman and italic for Hallmark, and has reeently completed Nofret roman and italie for Berthold (1986).

Although born and edueated in Switzerland, Frutiger\* has spent all his working life in Franee, where he went at the invitation of Charles Peignot of the Paris foundry Deberny & Peignot.

Like most foundries, Deberny & Peignot had grown by amalgamation, the result of a merger between the enterprise begun by Alexandre de Berny (the son of Balzac's mistress), then under the direction of Robert Girard, the designer of Astrée (1921), with the concern founded by Gustave Peignot in the 186os. Gustave Peignot died in 1889, and his son Georges initiated a spirited programme of type designs by contemporary graphie artists, first Eugéne Grasset, whose ugly type which bears his name (1898) was followed by those of Georges Auriol (1863-1938). Auriol (1901-4) is the only art nouveau face which can be read in long texts, and combined with the ornaments from the same hand in the books on typography by François Thibaudeau still looks fresh and lively. After this, Georges Peignot's later commissions were a disappointment, typified by Naudin (1913-20), based on the lettering of the illustrator Bernard Naudin. But he was responsible for the revival of the eighteenth-century Cochin and Moreau-le-Jeune types which were enthusiastically taken up in England and the United States, and for the first of the modern recuttings of the Imprimerie Nationale 'Garamond' (1912).

The Peignot dynasty was decimated in World War I when Georges and his three brothers were all killed. The management was taken over by Georges's son Charles (1897-1983), who reacted against the eighteenth century revival style in the direction of art déco modernism. The foundry was committed to Naudin and Astrée, and Peignot was too young to persuade it to change course overnight; but in the late 192os he commissioned the eminent poster artist A.

Charles Peignot was a type founder of vision, and he saw foundry type thrcatened not so much by mechanical hot metal composition as by filmsetting and dry transfer techniques. After World War II Deberny & Peignot anticipated the second challenge with a range of adhesive alphabet sheets ealled Typophane, for which Charles's son Rémy Peignot drew the attraetive titling Cristal (1957), and the first by securing French distribution rights to the Lumitype photosetting maehine (ealled the Photon in the United States), developed at thc end of the war by two French telephone engineers, Higonnet and Meyroud.

ln 1947 Charles Peignot and Cassandre collaborated on Touraine, a normalised version of Peignot made about the same time as Exeoffon's Chambord, but inereasingly Peignot turned his attention to faces for the Lumitype, whieh meant a repertoirc of faces not just for the Franeophile 'Gauloise' market, but for the world. With great foresight, he spotted in 1952 the aet and invited him to Paris.

Frutiger was born on 24 Mareh 1928 at Unterseen in Switzerland, the son of a weaver. As a child he used to help his father, and has always been coneerned with the way graphie techniques are grounded in the erafts, and the shapes of letters in the way they were seratehed, ineised, written or eut in metal. He showed an early interest in painting and seulpture, but his teachers at school prudently suggested hc put his lettering talents to use in the printing industry, and in 1944 he began a four year apprentieeship with the firm of Otto Sehaeffli in lnterlaken. At the same time he attended courses at the Zurieh school of arts and erafts, where his professors were Walter Käch and Alfred Willimann, and produeed as a school projeet an astonishingly aceomplished synopsis\* of the development of the western alphabet, with the speeimen seripts eut in the side grain of planks of wood. The work won a prize from the ministry of the interior, and an edition was printed from the wood. And while still

After his move to Paris in 1952, Frutiger soon produeed three faees for Deberny & Peignot whieh he later eame to regard as praetice work only, though many designers would be happy to rate them higher: they were a sharp-seriffed Latin titling fount, Président (1953), a shadowed sloped roman, Phoebus (1953) and a ealligraphie seript, Ondine (1954). Then, for the Lumitype, he supervised the adaptation of the elassie repertoire, Garamond, Baskerville, Bodoni and the rest, to the matrix disks, and also designed his first major faee, Méridien (1955), a sharp yet sensuous elassieal type whieh shows elearly both its ereator's abiding feeling for the ineised origins of roman lettering, and the eurious seriffed y whieh appears in several of his faees. Méridien was simultaneously eut in metal, in roman only.

When Peignot deeided to add a sans-serif family to the Lumitype range, Frutiger persuaded him to allow the development of the design he had begun at the Zurieh sehool, rather than adapt an existing type. Trials were made and approved, the produetion went ahead, and Univers appeared in 1957 in both film and foundry metal for hand setting; shortly afterwards

Monotype produeed it in both hot metal and film, and most typesetting systems followed.

The type's phenomenal sueeess was due to its skilfully unobtrusive detailing. It eliminated virtually everything but the essential forms of the letters, and was earefully and sweetly drawn. It was the most rational and basie of the post-war sans serifs. Max Miedinger's almost exaetly eontemporary Helvetiea for the Haas foundry in Basle (1957) returned to the Berthold Akzidenz/Standard family of the beginning of the eentury, which was still extremely popular among Swiss typographers, and polished it up. Antique Olive, designed a few years later by Roger Exeoffon, took the basie letters and gave them a strong dose of Gallie panaehe. Herman Zapf's 'serifless roman' Optima (1958), as the deseription suggests, pared away the serifs from a roman alphabet; and Syntax (1968), by another Swiss designer, Hans Meyer, is a eurious hybrid, taking some of the shapes of old styles and giving them a modified monoline treatment. Frutiger worked geometrieally, but his geometry is more eomplex and humane than that of the pre-war eonstrueted round-O sans-serifs such as Futura, and Univers runs more comfortably along the line as a result.

Frutiger devised an ingenious numerical coding system for the twenty-one members of the Univers family, to replace the oftcn confusing fount nomenclature of weights and widths usually adopted in type familics. Monotype, in making their version, took no notice, and kept to names and thcir own series numbers: thus Deberny & Pcignot Univers 83 appears as Monotype Univers Ultra Bold Expanded, Series 697. However, Frutiger used his system again with his later sans-serif, called after him.

The suecess of Univers led to many commissions, including a modified version of the face for use in signs at Orly airport at Paris. (A decade and a half later, this process worked in reverse, with airport lettering inspiring the typeface Frutiger.) In 1959 the face Opéra was designed for the slug-setting Sofratype machine, and the following year Egyptienne for Deberny & Peignot, the first of several versions of the mécane Egyptian letters. In addition, Frutiger worked on many book design and lettering jobs, largely for seientific publications, but he also produced a magnifieent sct of abstraet woodcut illustrations to the book of Genesis (1962), published as a fine edition by Pierre Bérès. In 1962 he set up his own studio, in association with Bruno Pfafli and André Gurtler.

The latter has since left to sct up Team 77 with Christian Mengelt and Erich Gsehwind, responsible for Media (1976), Signa (1978), Haas Unica (1980) and Basilia Haas (1982), as wcll as publishing much detailed analysis of typeface design.

Frutiger's next major face was commissioned by Monotype in 1962. After a transitional pcriod adapting metal types for use on the Monophoto, the Corporation asked for a new design, which was not cut in metal. Apollo is an historically interesting face, but not altogether a suceess: the italic is fidgety and the roman lacks a sense of flow. Frutiger has always been at his best not when he is designing classical alphabets but when taking normally unremarkable faees like sans-serifs and mécanc Egyptians and imbuing them with unexpeeted elegance.

This elegance can be seen in muted form in Serifa, designed for Bauer in 1967, and in the rather similar Glypha (1979); it is even apparent when he was given the thankless task of designing an alphabet which could be read by optieal character recognition machines, and still looked reasonably pleasant to humans. The result is OCR-B.

Iridium, designed in 1975 for Linotype, is a particularly handsome example of Frutiger's ability to

In 1784, The Marriage of Figaro' (Mariage de Figaro), by Beaumarchais, came out at Paris, where it was acted with astonishing success. Thomas Holcroft no sooner received notice of this piece, than he formed the instant resolution of going over to France to procure a copy of it, in order to translate and adapt it to the English stage.

He arrived in Paris the latter end of September, 1784, and proceeded to the lodgings of his friend Bonneville, to whom he immediately communicated the object of his journey. They both set about the accomplishment of it directly; but they found it attended with greater difficulty than they had expected. The comedy had not been printed: therefore, their first plan was to procure a manuscript copy, either at the theatre, or through some friend of the author. This attempt, however, they found fruitless, from the jealousy with which the managers of the French theatre prevented any copies from getting abroad. The only resource now remaining,

At the same timc as his work on Iridium, Frutiger was at work on lettcring for directional signs at the new Paris airport, Charles de Gaulle, at which the first terminal opened in 1975. Much of his work has becn in the non-typographical but related field of sign alphabets, notably for Electricité de Francc and Gaz de France. For Charles de Gaulle a sans-serif was chosen as being practical and scnsible, and a deliberate choice was made of a design that would not look as 'clinical' as the sans-serifs of the previous decades, such as Univers, and also would echo the curves of the terminal's architccturc. The result was codenamcd Roissy, after the village where the airport was built, and when adapted by Linotypc as a typefacc was called after its crcator. Frutiger (1976) is an cfficicnt but cheerful facc which shows that it is still possible to draw new forms of sans-serif. Yet, paradoxically, in spite of its origins in a modern airport, it has something of thc timeless about it, of the lettering scratched in stonc at thc dawn of script, and the universal sign language which Frutiger has studied so carefully.
