Icone, which Linotype issued in 198o, took his interest in thc glyphic formation of lettering a stage further: but here the details which transformed a sans-serif became thc letter itself. Versailles and Breughcl wcrc both designed for Linotype in 1982; the first is bascd on lettering cut in metal, and the second is like nothing so much as a spirited calligraphic rendering of Apollo, with a better sensc of flow and rhythm.

The grcat stroke of luck in my life', Frutiger wrote in 1964, 'is to have been blessed first with an artistic feeling for shapes, and sccond with an easy grasp of technical processes and of mathcmatics'. Since he wrotc that, the need for such a grasp has become ever more pressing. Like Zapf s, Frutigcr's understanding of the technical problems posed by the changes from pantographically cut metal type to film, and thcn to digitised faces, has ensured that his aesthctic vision has been realiscd with a minimum of loss by the way.

## Epilogue

The skelcton shapcs of thc lettcrs of our alphabct change hardly at all. Why then do skilled type designcrs dcvote so much timc, sometimes their whole lives, to drawing differcnt vcrsions of their outlines?

To thc qucstion put at thc beginning of this book, the case histories in it return a wide varicty of answers. Rudolf Koch, whose exalted view of thc craftsman's task was quoted in thc Introduction, and Eric Gill, werc both artist-craftsmen for whom type design provided a clcar solution to their doubts, which were widesprcad at the time, about thc rôle of the arts in socicty: letters werc not a luxury but a nccessity; thcir form followed thcir function; they werc, in Van Krimpcn's words, 'nothing but mcaning', and in Gill's, 'things, not pictures of things'. Thc making of letters has for many designers a strong mystical significance, whether thcy takc thc religious view that the word preceded the flesh, or the anthropological onc that thc making of graphic signs was onc of thc first activitics that distinguished men from bcasts.

Such a dcdication to the writing, drawing or cutting of letters does not, however, cxplain what makcs thc lettcrs diffcrcnt in outlinc. The study of the vast subject of the cultural influences on lctter forms absorbcd the last years of Stanley Morison and has occupied many scholars beforc and since, and is far from completc; but thc practical point is that dcsigners, not being cameras, change what they copy. In the coursc of an intcrcsting essay on the subject, Charles Bigclow neatly summed up the question. 'This is the erucial paradox of type dcsign imitation, and the forcc which drives type dcsign cvolution. A dcsigncr skilled and knowlcdgeable cnough to perccivc and rcndcr all the subtlc nuanccs of another master craftsman is really too good to do a slavish copy. A mastcr designer will inevitably transform an imitation into a creative act, which will givc the new design true individuality.'\*

Bram de Docs made the sensible observation which led to the dcsign of Trinité, that the adaptation of an existing type for a new typesetting system is in any casc so skillcd and laborious a process that a new design is preferable. Yet the question of what a 'ncw' design is rcmains a difficult one: it rcmains equally truc that all type designs are ncw, and that none is. Goudy wrote, 'It is hardly possible to create a good type face that will differ radically from the established forms of thc past', and yet his types arc more individual than many drawn by dcsigners who probably imagined that they wcre working with thcir own unaidcd wits. The creations which broke most abruptly with the forms of thc past were probably the constructed sans-serifs of the late 192os, and yet that break provcd a dead end.

In all this, type design is like the composition of music, in which themes are reused openly or covcrtly, and new material devcloped according to formal rules. No onc would drcam of censuring Brahms or Rachmaninov for writing works based on a theme of Paganini's, nor of claiming that Paganini was thereforc the greater composer. Although thc antecedents of a type may be interesting and revcaling, the type must be judged for what it is. The student should learn to discriminate bctween a copy and a reconstruction - in musical terms, between an arrangcmcnt and a set of variations  whilc bearing in mind that the copy may work bettcr on thc pagc than a morc original but awkward type.

As we havc seen, most theorists have taken the view that a text typcface should be quict and unassuming, without dctails which cry out; that, in Kcats's words, 'Heard melodies are sweet, but those unheard / Are sweeter'. And yet to type-conscious readers this is an impossibility: inevitably we become awarc of what a book is set in, and how wcll or badly it is set. It often begins early. I suspcct that many typographers feel an instinctivc revulsion from thc faces uscd in their school books: an carly employer of mine could not overcomc his dislike of Monotype Modern Scries 7 for this reason, and it has taken me a long while to appreciate Times New Roman. Quite apart from the larger question of whether rcaders are aware of the qualities of book production as a whole (most of the evidence says not, but I cannot bclieve it), thc legibility and beauty of thc typcface in which a book is sct has a mclody, which may bc swcet or grating, but can no longer be unheard. The loss of the state of innocencc in which Baskerville looks like Bembo, and Helvctica is indistinguishable from Univers, has the compensating advantage that we becomc more aware of thc tiny details and the 'subtle allurc' which go to make up thc best faces. It is some small acknowledgement we can make of thc labours of the designers.

![](images/69bef06daca277241e9ab46f85d380aaf69405839936b0657ea3a5e98352aeba.jpg)

![](images/3a745cc18a63518b0f2329a7921d2a94f0e9841ba6e649fcc6ac9296492bc0ee.jpg)

![](images/6e9877240603cfa1a6c87d4d34a630f080446d0494c1b8bd35d58d91bc2041fb.jpg)

Sebastian Carter was born in 1941, and educated at King's College, Cambridge, where he read English, Architecture and Fine Arts. After university he joined the London publisher John Murray, and in 1963 went to Paris to work for the Trianon Press, who produced the William Blake Trust facsimiles. After two years, he returned to London and divided his time between Ruari McLean Associates, The Stellar Press and freelance commissions, before joining his father Will Carter at the Rampant Lions Press in Cambridge. Here they concentrate on the design and printing of ephemera and limited editions, notably illustrated books in collaboration with Michael Rothenstein, the late Anthony Gross, and many other artists. In 1982 their work was the subject of a major retrospective exhibition at the Fitzwilliam Museum, Cambridge.

In 1971, Sebastian Carter won the first Francis Minns Award, given by the National Book League to a designer singled out by the judges at the annual British book design and production exhibition. His essay First principles of typography in the 197os was published by Bowaters in 1973, and his The book becomes: the making of a fine edition appeared in 1984; he has also contributed to Bogvennen, The Times literary supplement, Crafts and Matrix. In 19834 he was President of the Double Crown Club.

ISBN8008-7916

Printed in England
